==PAGE:5==
Abstract
Gait and balance impairments are frequent and disabling symptoms in individuals with
Parkinson’s disease (PD) and spinal cord injury (SCI), leading to increased risk of fall and
reduced quality of life. This exploratory randomized controlled crossover trial evaluated
the feasibility and potential efficacy of vibration-based intelligent insoles (Path Feel, Walk
With Path) in improving gait, balance, and sensory perception in PD and SCI popula-
tions. Clinical outcomes (Unified Parkinsons Disease Rating Scale, Berg Balance Scale,
Mini Balance Evaluation Systems Test, Qualitative Sensitivity Test), insole-derived gait
parameters, and patient-reported measures (questionnaires) were collected. In addition,
qualitative feedback from participants and clinicians was collected regarding both the trial
experience and the usability of the device.
Linear mixed-effects models were used to analyze immediate and long-term effects. A to-
tal of 25 participants (12 PD, 13 SCI) completed both control and intervention conditions,
wearing haptic feedback insoles for 7 days (PD) or 14 days (SCI). PD participants showed
significant immediate improvements in balance (Mini Balance Evaluation Systems Test:
+8.9 points) and long-term gains in daily motor function (Unified Parkinson’s Disease Rat-
ing Scale Part II: −3 points). SCI participants showed trends toward improved gait speed
and balance, although some variability increased. Most other outcomes lacked statistical
power (<30%) due to high inter- and intra-subject variability and the small sample size.
Feasibility outcomes were mixed: while adherence was acceptable, technical challenges,
including Bluetooth instability, inconsistent vibration, and battery issues, frequently dis-
rupted data collection and impacted user experience. Some participants reported dis-
comfort due to noise or fit. Nevertheless, the study was successfully conducted in a
clinical environment, with effective interdisciplinary collaboration. These findings support
the potential of vibrating insoles as a rehabilitation tool and highlight key considerations
for improving their design and implementation in future trials.

Acknowledgements
I would like to express my sincere gratitude to everyone who supported me throughout
the completion of this thesis.
First and foremost, I would like to thank Anders Stockmarr, my supervisor at DTU, for his
expert guidance and support in the statistical design and analysis. His insightful input has
been crucial to this work.
A deep thank you goes to Trine Hørmann Thomsen, RN, PhD, for her clinical supervision,
her role in shaping the clinical trial design, guiding the ethical submission process, and
supporting participant recruitment and data collection. I am especially grateful for the
many opportunities she has offered me throughout my academic journey, which have
nurtured my growing passion for clinical trials, a field I am eager to explore further.
I also thank Henrik Hagen Poder for his help with participant recruitment and data collec-
tion sessions, and for his constant motivation and dedication to the project. His enthusi-
asm and encouragement were a constant source of energy and support, both profession-
ally and personally.
To my clinical colleagues and friends, thank you for the shared discussions, collaboration,
and moral support that made this experience both educational and enjoyable.
Finally, I want to extend my warmest thanks to all the participants in the study. Your
patience, enthusiasm, and openness not only contributed meaningfully to the project, but
also made my journey of learning Danish fun and culturally enriching.

Glossary
Control The baseline or standard condition against which a new treatment or intervention
is compared. Controls may receive no treatment, a placebo, or standard care to help
isolate the effect of the intervention. 11
Crossover design A clinical trial design where participants receive multiple interventions
sequentially, with a washout period in between. This allows each participant to act
as their own control, improving the reliability of comparisons between treatments.
2, 12, 29
Dependent variable A variable whose value is measured in an experiment and is influ-
enced or predicted by changes in one or more independent variables. 29
Fixed effect An effect that is constant across individuals or experimental units, repre-
senting the average influence of a variable in a statistical model. 29
Gait The characteristic way a person walks, including rhythm, speed, and movement
patterns. 1
H&Y scale Hoehn and Yahr scale; a clinical tool used to describe the progression of
Parkinson’s Disease symptoms on a scale from 1 (mild, unilateral involvement) to 5
(wheelchair-bound or bedridden unless aided). 25
Haptic feedback A sensory communication method using touch or vibrations to provide
feedback to the user.
In wearable technology, such as vibrating insoles, haptic
feedback can cue movement or improve sensory perception. 2
Independent variable A variable that is manipulated or categorized to observe its effect
on the dependent variable. 29
Intervention The specific treatment or action being tested in a study, intended to produce
a beneficial effect, such as improved gait or balance in this context. 11
Power analysis A statistical method used to determine the sample size needed to detect
an effect of a given size with a specified level of confidence. 34
Random effect An effect that varies across individuals or units, used in statistical models
to account for variation due to grouping or clustering (e.g., subjects). 29
Randomized controlled trial A study design in which participants are randomly allo-
cated to different treatment groups. This approach reduces bias and increases the
validity of conclusions about the efficacy or safety of interventions. 2
SPIRIT diagram A standardized visual representation of the schedule for participant en-
rollment, interventions, and assessments throughout a clinical trial. It helps ensure
transparency and clarity in trial protocols and timelines. 13
viii
iGRASP
==PAGE:11==
Statistical power Residual effects from a prior treatment period that persist and may in-
fluence outcomes in subsequent treatment periods within a crossover study. Iden-
tifying and minimizing carryover is important to ensure valid results. 71
Treatment Effect Parameter A parameter that quantifies the causal impact of a treat-
ment or intervention on an outcome variable. 29
WISCI score Walking Index for Spinal Cord Injury; a scale used to assess walking ability
in individuals with spinal cord injury, based on the amount of physical assistance
and devices required for walking 10 meters. 25
Within-subjects design An experimental design in which the same participants are ex-
posed to all conditions or treatments, allowing direct comparison within individuals
and reducing variability due to individual differences. 2

1
Introduction
This section presents the study context, problem statement, objectives, and an overview
of the project.
1.1
Research Context and Rationale
Gait is defined as the pattern and manner in which an individual walks and is an important
indicator of mobility and overall health. It can be described using key parameters such
as walking speed, step length, and rhythm, which reflect how smoothly and efficiently a
person moves. Changes in these parameters can reveal issues related to balance, coor-
dination, or neurological conditions. Measuring gait accurately is essential for diagnosing
problems and evaluating treatments, especially in populations affected by diseases like
Parkinson’s disease (PD) and spinal cord injury (SCI).
PD and SCI significantly alter gait and balance, increasing fall risk. In Europe, PD affects
about 1.2 million people, and SCI over 1.5 million [1, 2]. These conditions cause sensory
and motor deficits that compromise coordination and stability, requiring effective rehabili-
tation. While traditional methods like gait training offer some benefits, interest is growing
in wearable technologies to further enhance outcomes. Vibration-equipped insoles, worn
inside regular or specialized footwear can enhance sensory feedback and support gait
correction. However, their use in PD and SCI populations remains limited.
Intelligent insoles also enable continuous, objective gait monitoring in real-life settings.
As shown in Figure 1.1, they are used in applications ranging from fall prevention in older
adults to diabetic foot care [3]. These devices incorporate various sensing technologies
(capacitive, piezoresistive, piezoelectric, and resistive) leading to a broad range of sensor-
based insole products.
Figure 1.1: Insole technologies for gait monitoring [3].
iGRASP
1
==PAGE:18==
This study explores the feasibility and potential of vibrational haptic feedback insoles to
enhance motor control, gait, and balance in individuals with PD and SCI. It uses the Path
Feel insoles developed by Walk With Path (WWP), featuring pre-established pressure
sensor placement and vibration frequency settings. A randomized controlled trial (RCT)
with a within-subjects design and crossover design compares baseline measurements
with those collected after insole use.
Outcomes are evaluated using clinical gait and balance scales, quality-of-life question-
naires, and features extracted from insole sensor data. The study aims to inform fu-
ture rehabilitation strategies by evaluating vibration-based gait therapy and continuous
gait monitoring. Throughout the study, gait and balance are examined together, as they
represent complementary aspects of mobility: gait refers to dynamic movement during
walking, while balance concerns the body’s ability to maintain stability, particularly during
stationary or transitional postures. Statistical analysis is applied to evaluate changes and
intervention effects.
This master’s thesis is conducted in collaboration with Rigshospitalet Glostrup Hospital,
where participant experiments take place at the Movement Disorder Clinic. A Gantt chart
outlining the project timeline is presented in Appendix A.1. Ethical approval from the Vi-
denskabsetisk Medicinsk Komité (VMK) was obtained, and the Trial Master File is main-
tained per ICH guidelines.
1.2
Problem statement
The research question of this clinical trial is as follows:
Could vibrational haptic feedback insoles improve functional gait ability and balance in
individuals with PD and SCI during both immediate and long-term use in real-world set-
tings?
1.3
Objectives and Methodological Approach
This exploratory study investigates the potential benefits of wearing Path Feel insoles,
while evaluating their short- and long-term feasibility. It also assesses the practicality of
running a technology-driven clinical trial, including gait measurement, recruitment, data
collection, and insole adherence, offering insights into real-world use of wearable gait-
monitoring tools.
Primary Objectives
• For PD patients: Assess improvements in functional walking ability, balance, and
reduction in Freezing of Gait (FoG) following immediate and long-term (7 days) use
of Path Feel insoles.
• For SCI patients: Evaluate improvements in functional walking ability, balance,
and sensory abilities following immediate and long-term (14 days) use of Path Feel
insoles.
Secondary Objectives
• Impact on Quality of Life: Evaluate the effect of Path Feel insoles on patients’
overall quality of life, using standardized questionnaires.
• Impact of Daily Vibration Use on Improvement: Determine whether increased
daily use of the vibrating insoles leads to better improvements in gait, balance, and
sensory abilities.
2
iGRASP
==PAGE:19==
Feasibility Objectives
• Feasibility Assessment: Evaluate the practicality of implementing Path Feel in-
soles in clinical settings, including patient adherence and challenges related to wear-
ing the insoles for the required period.
• Measurement Evaluation: Assess the suitability of tools and methods used for gait,
balance, and quality of life data collection to identify the most effective approaches
for future research.
• Data Management: Optimize data collection and analysis processes to ensure ac-
curacy and efficiency for subsequent studies.
1.3.1
Risk Assessment and Anticipated Challenges of the Study
While the feasibility outcomes are presented in detail later in this report, this subsection
highlights the key risks and practical challenges identified during the study design phase
and a pilot study. These insights are crucial for understanding potential limitations and
trying to overcome some possible issues.
Session coordination: Managing and aligning schedules of clinicians, patients, investi-
gators, and statisticians is key to smooth data collection during the 3-month study.
Medical device tested: Ensuring enough functional insoles are available and tested be-
fore the study is vital to avoid malfunctions during the intervention.
Statistical concerns: Multiple confounders (age, BMI, disease stage, baseline balance,
medication, fatigue) must be accounted for, balancing scientific rigor with clinical trial re-
alities and patient safety.
1.3.2
Literature Review Methodology and Journal Selection
The keywords used to identify relevant literature for this review included: Gait, PD, SCI,
Insoles, Balance, Posture, Gait, Rehabilitation, Wearable, Pressure Sensors, IMU, FoG,
Postural/Adaptive/Intelligent/Smart Insoles. These keywords were combined using AND/OR
operators to refine the search.
Journals Used: Literature was sourced primarily from PubMed, ScienceDirect, and Na-
ture, focusing on gait disturbances and rehabilitation in PD and SCI.
Research Tools: Google Scholar, DTU Findit, Connected Papers, and Research Rabbit
were used to identify and map relevant literature.
Journal Selection: Journals were chosen based on acceptance rates, Journal Impact
Factor, and CiteScore to ensure high quality and scientific relevance.
1.4
Thesis Structure
This thesis presents a clinical investigation of vibrational haptic feedback insoles. Each
chapter builds a cohesive narrative from background to conclusions.
Chapter 2: Literature Review
Outlines gait impairments in PD and SCI, reviews rehabilitation methods, and introduces
vibrational haptic feedback and relevant outcome measures.
Chapter 3: Materials and Methods
Describes the study design, insole technology, participant recruitment, clinical assess-
ments, and the crossover experimental setup.
iGRASP
3
==PAGE:20==
Chapter 4: Data Overview
Summarizes the collected dataset, characterizes the study population, and provides con-
text for analysis.
Chapter 5: Data Analysis Methods
Details statistical tools including linear mixed-effects models and hypothesis testing to
assess intervention effects.
Chapter 6: Clinical Trial Results
Presents study outcomes, focusing on immediate and short-term effects on gait and bal-
ance.
Chapter 7: Feasibility Outcomes
Reports on participant adherence, technical performance of the insoles, and challenges
related to clinical implementation.
Chapter 8: Discussion and Conclusion
Summarizes findings, discusses clinical relevance and study limitations, and suggests
future directions for wearable gait rehabilitation.

iGRASP
==PAGE:21==
2
Background and Literature Review
This section outlines PD and SCI, traditional gait analysis, vibration-based insole thera-
pies, and includes a preliminary sample size estimate based on literature review.
2.1
Parkinson’s Disease and Spinal Cord Injury
2.1.1
Parkinson’s Disease and Symptoms
PD is a progressive neurodegenerative disorder affecting both motor and non-motor func-
tions. Hallmark motor symptoms include bradykinesia, tremor, and rigidity, with freezing
of gait (FoG) being one of the most disabling manifestations. FoG, experienced by more
than half of PD patients in advanced stages, is characterized by sudden episodes of move-
ment cessation, often accompanied by akinesia or trembling knees, increasing falling risk
[4, 5].
Cueing strategies, such as auditory or tactile stimuli, have been developed to help miti-
gate FoG episodes. Traditional techniques involve external rhythmic cues like counting,
clapping, or tapping to prompt movement. A more recent approach involves vibrational
haptic feedback delivered through insoles, which aims to enhance proprioceptive input
and support gait initiation [6].
Managing PD-related gait disturbances remains challenging due to symptom fluctuations
and the limitations of current therapies. Pharmacological treatments provide temporary
symptom relief, but efficacy varies, particularly for gait impairments. Deep Brain Stimu-
lation (DBS) is an option in advanced cases but is invasive and not always effective for
FoG. Physical therapy can offer benefits, yet outcomes are inconsistent. Furthermore,
inter-rater variability in clinical assessments underscores the need for objective, continu-
ous monitoring tools.
2.1.2
Spinal Cord Injury and Symptoms
SCI results from trauma or degenerative conditions that damage the spinal cord, leading
to varying degrees of sensory and motor impairment. The extent of disability depends
on the injury level and severity, with incomplete SCIs preserving some movement and
sensation. Rehabilitation, assistive devices, and surgical interventions aim to improve
mobility, though they often fall short in fully restoring gait and balance [7].
Unlike PD, where symptoms progress gradually, SCI-related impairments are often im-
mediate and recovery is highly dependent on early intervention and rehabilitation. SCI
patients commonly experience difficulties in maintaining posture, executing smooth gait
cycles, and responding to sensory feedback, increasing their risk of fall episodes. Current
treatment modalities, including physical therapy and pharmacotherapy, provide partial
functional recovery but lack continuous, adaptive support [8].
2.2
Conventional Gait and Balance Assessment Methods
Gait analysis is a valuable tool for assessing physical and cognitive health, as gait patterns
reflect underlying conditions such as disease, aging, and activity level [9, 10, 11]. It is
used to diagnose movement disorders, track rehabilitation progress, predict fall risk, and
evaluate athletic performance [12, 13].
iGRASP
5
==PAGE:22==
In neurological disorders like PD and SCI, gait is traditionally assessed using clinical
scales such as the Berg Balance Scale (BBS), Mini-BESTest, or Timed Up and Go (TUG).
While informative, these methods are episodic, subjective, and require trained clinicians.
Motion capture systems and instrumented treadmills offer greater precision but are costly,
time-consuming, and confined to clinical settings [14]. Moreover, gait in individuals with
PD is highly variable, particularly due to fluctuations between ON and OFF medication
states [15]. This variability limits the reliability of single-time-point assessments in captur-
ing overall gait performance.
Altogether, existing methods lack continuity, scalability, and objectivity. These limitations
reduce their ability to track meaningful changes over time or adapt to real-world variability.
There is a pressing need for wearable, low-burden technologies that enable long-term,
real-world gait monitoring, offering a more accurate, accessible, and responsive approach
to evaluating gait performance beyond clinical environments.
2.3
Intelligent Insoles and Vibrational Therapies for Gait
Analysis
2.3.1
Continuous Gait Analysis
Wearable sensors like pressure-sensing insoles allow continuous, real-world gait moni-
toring, providing quantitative data such as stride length and cycle duration [16, 17]. Their
applications range from fall detection and diabetic foot assessments to FoG detection in
PD, and gait monitoring in both PD and SCI [3].
Typically, these insoles include pressure sensors and an Inertial Measurement Unit (IMU),
enabling segmentation of gait events 5Heel Strike, Toe Strike, Heel Off, and Toe Off (Fig-
ure 2.1, Appendix Figure A.1). Temporal metrics (e.g., cycle duration, cadence) reflect
coordination, while spatial ones (e.g., stride length, turning) capture movement efficiency.
Figure 2.1: Gait parameters traditionally used in gait analysis (adapted from [12]).
6
iGRASP
==PAGE:23==
Metrics are validated against gold standards like Vicon and grouped into five domains:
rhythm, pace, asymmetry, variability, and postural control [18, 19] (Figure A.2). Sensor
placement can be tailored to study aims [20], and advanced analytics, including machine
learning, enhance clinical insight [21].
2.3.2
Vibrational Therapies and Vibrational Haptic Feedback Insoles
Vibrational therapies use haptic stimulation to enhance sensory feedback and improve
motor function.
Haptic refers to the sense of touch; in this context, it describes the use of vibration to
provide tactile feedback, enabling users to perceive and respond to sensory input. These
vibratory cues have been shown to reduce FoG in individuals with PD and enhance move-
ment initiation and postural stability. By mimicking natural sensory input, the vibrations
engage neural circuits involved in motor control and balance. This sensory stimulation
activates the brain’s motor centers, thereby supporting improved gait and stability.
This approach leverages the established connection between sensory input and motor
output in both PD and SCI populations, while potentially reducing cognitive load during
ambulation. It represents a personalized and innovative strategy to improve mobility and
functional independence in affected individuals.
Numerous studies support the therapeutic potential of vibration delivered through insoles,
referred to in this study as vibrational haptic feedback insoles. This technique has shown
promise in enhancing balance, gait, and postural control across various populations, in-
cluding older adults [22], individuals with muscle fatigue [23], patients with diabetic pe-
ripheral neuropathy [6], and athletes [24]. These findings suggest a neurophysiological
foundation for vibration-based interventions, which may offer similar benefits for individu-
als with PD and SCI.
Based on the context and literature review, a sample size estimation was performed be-
fore trial design to determine the minimum number of participants needed to detect a
meaningful intervention effect and guide study planning.
2.4
Sample Size Estimation
Sample size was estimated via power analysis using R’s pwr.t.test, targeting 80%
power and 5% significance, with a 15% dropout rate. Velocity was the primary outcome
for both PD ans SCI groups. Balance was assessed with Mini-BESTest (PD) and Berg
Balance Scale (BBS) (SCI).
A paired t-test was chosen for its simplicity and conservative estimate despite more com-
plex alternatives [25, 26, 27]. Table 2.1 shows required sample sizes based on effect
sizes (Cohen’s d) from literature.
Table 2.1: Sample sizes to detect changes in gait and balance parameters.
Parameter
Effect Size (d)
Sample Size (n)
BBS (SCI)
0.41[28, 29]
48
Velocity (SCI)
0.28[29]
102
Velocity (PD)
0.449[30]
40
Mini-BESTest (PD)
0.107[31]
687
Smaller effect sizes require larger samples; e.g., Mini-BESTest (PD) needs 687 partici-
pants, while velocity (PD) requires 40, guiding efficient trial design.

3
Materials and Methods
This section presents the data collection procedures and trial methods used in the study.
3.1
Description of WWP Insoles and the Intervention Protocol
WWP insoles were chosen for this study due to their existing collaboration with the hos-
pital and their unique position as the only Danish provider of insoles delivering vibrational
feedback. These insoles integrate vibrational haptic feedback and gait monitoring capa-
bilities, which are critical for this project.
Each participant wore two WWP insoles (one per foot) in active Custom Test mode, sam-
pling at 200Hz.
Data from 9 pressure sensors and an IMU per foot were Bluetooth-
transmitted to a smartphone, then uploaded anonymously to Walk With Path’s private
cloud, Path Insight. Raw data from the insoles includes the recording start time, sampling
frequency, and measurements for both feet in the form of a JSON file. This is further
described in the Appendix, Table B.1.
Figure 3.1: Walk With Path insoles.
Using the IMU and pressure data gait cycles were segmented into steps. Key events, such
as Initial Contact, MidStart, MidEnd, Foot Off, Toe Strike, and Heel Off, were identified.
From these events, temporal and spatial features were extracted, as discussed in Section
2.3.1 of the literature review:
• Spatial statistics: Stride length (m), step length (m), stride velocity (m/s), peak an-
gular velocity (deg/s), swing speed (m/s), strike angle (degrees), lift-off angle (de-
grees), 3D path length (m), turning (degrees)
• Temporal statistics: Cycle duration (s), cadence (steps/min), stance phase (% of
gait cycle), swing phase (% of gait cycle), loading response (% of stance), foot-flat
phase (% of stance), pushing phase (% of stance), double support phase (% of gait
cycle)
For each walking test and both feet, statistics including mean, median, standard deviation,
interquartile range, maximum, minimum, asymmetry ratio, and coefficient of variation (CV)
were extracted. Means, CVs, and asymmetry ratios were used per foot following prior
recommendations [32, 27, 33]. The CV was calculated separately for the left and right
sides using the formula:
iGRASP
9
==PAGE:26==
CVL,R = σL,R
µL,R
where σ and µ denote the standard deviation and the mean, respectively, for the left (L) or
right (R) foot. This provides a normalized measure of variability for each gait parameter.
Asymmetry was computed using the Robinson index, defined as:
Asymmetry Index = 100 ×
µL −µR
0.5 × (µL + µR)
This index quantifies the relative difference between limbs. A value of 0 indicates perfect
symmetry, while larger absolute values reflect greater asymmetry between the left and
right limbs. This method was considered reliable since the study participants generally
do not exhibit a distinct affected and unaffected leg [33].
Initially, for each gait feature those five metrics were initially extracted: mean and CV
for both left and right foot, and the asymmetry ratio. However, as shown later, left and
right values can be averaged without loss of information. Hence, three variables per
feature were retained for analysis: overall mean, CV, and asymmetry ratio. Since walking
tests are traditionally repeated three times for improved accuracy, the recordings were
concatenated before feature extraction.
A subset of spatial and temporal gait parameters were selected based on their robustness
(validated with Vicon data), clinical relevance, and support in the literature for assessing
gait in elderly, PD, and SCI populations [34, 35, 36, 37, 27, 38]. These features were
grouped by gait domains (see Section 2.3.1):
• Pace: Stride velocity, swing speed, and gait speed.
• Spatial: Step length and 3D path length.
• Rhythm: Cadence and cycle duration.
• Postural control: Stance, swing and strike angle.
• Variability: Asymmetry ratio for each gait parameter.
The intervention tested in this study involved vibrational haptic feedback from WWP in-
soles. Two actuators were placed at the front and rear of each insole and delivered vi-
brations between 100–220 Hz. This was modulated in response to local pressure sensor
input.
3.2
Participants Recruitment Strategy
Participants were recruited through the Department of Brain and Spinal Cord Injuries
and the Movement Disorder Clinic at Rigshospitalet Glostrup. Attending physicians and
nurses informed potential participants about the study, provided written information re-
garding study procedures and participant involvement, and obtained consent to forward
their contact information to the research team. The target enrollment was 30 participants:
15 with SCI and 15 with PD. All detailed inclusion and exclusion criteria are provided in
Appendix B.3. A summary of the key inclusion criteria is:
• Diagnosis of Parkinson’s disease or incomplete Spinal Cord Injury
• Age over 18 years
10
iGRASP
==PAGE:27==
• Experiencing motor symptoms such as balance, gait, or postural disturbances, in-
cluding Freezing of Gait (FoG) for PD or sensory symptoms for SCI
• Ability to travel to the hospital independently or with assistance from a spouse or
caregiver
• Willingness to wear intelligent insoles with slight vibrations
• Ability to understand the participant information document and communicate in En-
glish or Danish
Demographic and clinical data, including age, gender, height, weight, shoe size, and dis-
ease characteristics, were collected at baseline during the first meeting (Day 0). A sum-
mary table presenting the mean and standard deviation of each demographic and clinical
characteristic is provided later in Section 4.
3.3
Gait and Balance Assessment Methods
Based on the literature review (Section 2.2) and discussions with clinicians, the most rel-
evant and commonly used questionnaires and gait evaluation tests were selected. Stan-
dardized scoring methods were applied, and scores were compared between control and
intervention periods. A detailed explanation of each test is provided in Appendix B.4, with
a summary following.
3.3.1
Assessments for Parkinson’s Disease
For PD, the selected assessments were:
• UPDRS II and III [39] – Completed by the clinician, the Unified Parkinson’s Disease
Rating Scale is a validated tool for evaluating PD symptoms, treatment effects, and
progression. The template can be found in the Appendix B.4.2.
Part II assesses daily activities (score range: 0–52).
Part III focuses on motor symptoms (score range: 0–132). Only items 7–14 were
analyzed. Lower scores indicate better health.
• PDQ-39 [40] – Completed by the participant, this validated questionnaire evaluates
quality of life in PwP. To reduce burden from repeated completion, only items 1–
10 (Mobility) and 37–39 (Bodily Discomfort) were analyzed. Lower scores indicate
better perceived health.
• Mini BESTest [41] – Completed by the clinician, this test assesses balance across
four domains: anticipatory and reactive postural control, sensory orientation, and
dynamic gait. Each of the 14 items is scored 0–2; higher scores reflect better bal-
ance. The template can be found in Appendix B.4.2.
• Number of FoG per Session – Recorded by the clinician during assessments.
• Daily FoG Questionnaire – Completed by the participant
Participants recorded daily FoG episodes and shared feedback on their health and
the insoles, this way helping to track changes over time.
3.3.2
Assessments for Spinal Cord Injury
For SCI, the assessments included:
iGRASP
11
==PAGE:28==
• BBS [42] – Completed by the clinician, the BBS evaluates balance and functional
mobility through 14 tasks each scored 0–4 (total: /56). Higher scores indicate better
function. The template is attached in Appendix B.4.2.
• QST (MPT, MDT, VDT) [42, 43, 44] – Completed by the clinician, QST assesses
sensory thresholds relevant to mechanoreception and vibration. These are the ar-
eas most likely influenced by the insoles.
MPT was tested using pinprick stimulators, MDT with Von Frey filaments, and VDT
using a tuning fork (Figure B.1). Six outcomes were analyzed: MDT-right/left, MPT-
right/left, and VDT-right/left.
(a) Pinprick (MPT) and tuning fork (VDT).
(b) Von Frey filaments (MDT).
Figure 3.2: QST tools used for SCI participants.
• SF-36 [45] – Completed by the participant, this questionnaire assesses health-
related quality of life via 36 items and helps monitor health and treatment impact.
The total score and three dimensions: physical functioning, role limitations (physi-
cal), and bodily pain were analyzed. Higher scores indicate better health.
3.4
Experiment Protocol and Design
The overall study design was shaped through interdisciplinary collaboration, incorporating
input from clinicians, previous literature, and practical considerations from preliminary
testing. This study employs a randomized controlled trial (RCT) with a crossover design
to evaluate both the immediate and long-term effects of a vibration-based intervention
[46].
In a crossover design, participants are randomly assigned to begin with either the vibra-
tion intervention condition (denoted I) or the control condition (denoted C), in which the
insoles are worn without activating the vibrations for the same duration (Figure 3.3). Con-
sequently, participants follow one of two possible sequences: CI (Control period followed
by Intervention period) or IC (Intervention period followed by Control period). These an-
notations are used consistently throughout this report.
The crossover design was selected to reduce inter-subject variability by allowing each
12
iGRASP
==PAGE:29==
participant to act as their own control. This approach increases statistical power and
improves the precision of effect estimates, especially in studies with limited sample sizes.
It is particularly relevant given the small and heterogeneous populations with PD and SCI,
where between-subject variability is high and recruitment is challenging.
Figure 3.3: Two-period, Two-sequence crossover design.
Including a control period, where the insoles are worn without vibration, is essential for
isolating the specific effects of the vibration-based stimulation. Without this condition, ob-
served changes could be attributed to placebo effects, participant expectations, increased
attention during the trial, or simply wearing the device. The control condition ensures that
any improvement can be confidently attributed to the vibration feature itself, rather than
to external or non-specific factors.
It is important to note that the study is not blinded, as the vibrations produced by the med-
ical device are perceivable and cannot be concealed. This lack of blinding may introduce
bias, both in participant behavior and in clinician evaluations.
Data Collection and Assessments
The following SPIRIT diagram (Figure 3.4) illustrates the trial timeline.
Data was collected at three time points: baseline t1 (meeting 1), post-period 1 t2 (meeting
2), and post-period 2 t3 (meeting 3). At each time point participants completed a quality-of-
life questionnaire related to gait and balance, which generated a specific score. Clinicians
also assessed QST for SCI participants and UPDRS for PD participants, as shown in
Figure 3.4.
During each meeting, standardized assessments were performed twice, with and without
vibration, following the Standard Operating Procedure (Appendix Figure B.2):
• PD participants: PDQ-39, UPDRS, Mini BESTest, and three trials of the 10-meter
walk test (10MWT).
• SCI participants: SF-36, QST, BBT, and three trials of 10MWT.
One clinician assessed PD participants, and another assessed SCI participants to reduce
variability from subjective grading differences.
Qualitative feedback on insole comfort, charging, app usability, and intervention experi-
ence (vibration perception, benefit, confidence) was collected via brief interviews during
and after sessions. Adverse events and dropouts were recorded for safety and analysis.
All qualitative data were manually noted by the investigator and will be analyzed using
standard methods (see Section 5.3).
iGRASP
13
==PAGE:30==
Figure 3.4: SPIRIT diagram of the iGRASP study. tia denotes the data collection time
point for session i conducted without vibration. tib denotes the data collection time point
for session i conducted with vibration.
Analysis Strategy
Immediate effects are evaluated by comparing same-day recordings: t1a vs t1b, t2a vs
t2b, and t3a vs t3b. For each participant, the effect of the intervention is estimated by
computing the within-meeting difference in measured parameters between the conditions
with and without intervention. This approach isolates the direct, short-term impact of the
intervention.
Long-term effects are assessed by examining changes in parameter values across the
three study visits. Specifically, two intervals are considered: from t1a to t2a, and from
t2a to t3a. These intervals reflect the cumulative effect of wearing the insoles for ap-
proximately 1–2 weeks, with or without the intervention, depending on the participant’s
assigned sequence in the crossover design. Comparisons are performed on recordings
without vibrations (t1a, t2a, and t3a). Statistical analyses are applied to the computed
differences to assess long-term effects.
As a summary, Figure 3.5 presents the overall structure of the iGRASP analysis.
Intervention Period
For PD participants, the intervention lasted approximately 7 days, adjustable for individual
schedules. SCI participants had a 14-day period to allow observable QST changes. The
shorter PD duration accounted for the added burden of daily FoG questionnaires. These
durations were based on prior studies and clinical judgment.
14
iGRASP
==PAGE:31==
Figure 3.5: iGRASP Analysis Structure: immediate Effect and PD vs SCI Subgroup Anal-
yses with Confounders and Outcomes Highlighted (Grey boxes).
The crossover design assumed no carryover effects, supported by evidence that vibration
feedback effects do not persist. Therefore, no washout period was included, but carryover
will be tested statistically before final analysis.
Clinical Trial Endpoints
Based on the study objectives outlined in Section 1.3, the primary and secondary end-
points of this clinical trial are defined as following:
Primary Endpoints
• PD Cohort
– Immediate Effect: Change in gait parameters (e.g., step length, cadence, ve-
locity) and clinical balance score (MiniBESTest) between control and interven-
tion conditions.
– Long-Term Effect (7 days): Change in gait parameters and MiniBESTest score
from baseline to post-intervention.
– Freezing of Gait (FoG): Change in FoG frequency or severity based on self-
report (e.g., UPDRS-II item) or clinical observation.
• SCI Cohort
– Immediate Effect: Change in gait parameters (e.g., step length, cadence, ve-
locity) and balance score (Berg Balance Scale) between control and interven-
tion conditions.
– Long-Term Effect (14 days): Change in gait parameters and BBS score from
baseline to post-intervention.
iGRASP
15
==PAGE:32==
– Sensory Function: Change in clinical sensory assessment scores (e.g., QST)
following the intervention.
Secondary Endpoints
• Quality of Life: Change in PDQ-39 scores for PD participants and SF-36 scores
for SCI participants from baseline to post-intervention.
• Usage-Effect Relationship: Association between daily insole usage duration and
improvements in gait, balance, or sensory outcomes.
Exploratory and Feasibility Endpoints
Moreover, the feasibility endpoints are:
• Feasibility and Acceptability
– Qualitative feedback from patients and clinicians on the usability, comfort, and
integration of the Path Feel insoles in daily life.
– Quantitative analysis of daily insole usage throughout the trial period to assess
participant compliance.
• Validation of Tools and Procedures: Evaluation of the utility of gait analysis (e.g.,
10MWT), clinical scores (MiniBESTest, BBS, sensory tests), and QoL question-
naires (PDQ-39, SF-36).
• Trial Implementation Assessment: Identification of practical challenges during the
trial (e.g., device failures, data issues, scheduling difficulties) and recommendations
for future studies.
3.5
Data Management and Ethical Considerations
Any physical documents (e.g., questionnaires, trial master file) were securely stored at
the study collection site and managed via REDCap (Research Electronic Data Capture).
Informed consent was obtained from all participants, and data were anonymized to ensure
confidentiality. The data were managed as follows:
• Gait data: After being uploaded to a secure drive managed by the WWP company,
the resulting .json files were then downloaded onto a local server for analysis.
• Clinical, demographic, and official data: Collected and stored using the REDCap
software. For each participant, a unique ID was generated, and a corresponding
record was created, as illustrated in Figure 3.6. Six instruments were developed
for each cohort: schedule form, demographic data, clinical data (10MWT velocity,
general comments, and observations), questionnaires (PDQ-39 / SF-36) and scores
(UPDRS and Mini-BESTest / QST and BBS).
The first three instruments were completed only at baseline, while the remaining three
were filled out at each meeting. An overview of all records and instruments is available
through the REDCap interface, as shown in Figure 3.7, allowing for the identification and
management of missing data or entry errors.
The trial posed minimal risks, managed by predefined procedures. Non-invasive devices
did not interfere with participants, and no side effects were reported. Safety measures
16
iGRASP
==PAGE:33==

in REDCap by record ID for PD participants.
(b) Structure of data collection instruments
in REDCap by record ID for SCI participants.
Figure 3.6: Overview of data collection setup in REDCap for both cohorts.
Figure 3.7: iGRASP REDCap Record Status Dashboard. Red dots represent incomplete
instruments.
included healthcare professional assistance during assessments and strict stopping rules
to prevent falls.
The potential benefits for motor function in PD and SCI justified the minimal risks. The
study complied with the Declaration of Helsinki, ICH GCP guidelines, and Danish law,
with protocol approval from relevant authorities. Protocol deviations were reported to the
sponsor.
Personal and health data were protected under all applicable regulations. Participants did
not receive financial incentives but were covered by public insurance.

==PAGE:36==
The table provides a description of each measured outcome, including units (where appli-
cable), the annotation used for statistical modeling, and the expected direction of change if
the intervention proves beneficial (when applicable). These anticipated trends are grounded
in prior empirical studies and theoretical frameworks related to gait improvement and func-
tional capacity [36, 47, 48, 49, 50]. The previously introduced Figure 2.1 visually illustrates
each gait feature for added clarity.
The expected trajectories of the mean values vary depending on the outcome measure.
In general, gait asymmetry ratios are hypothesized to decrease toward zero, indicating
improved symmetry between limbs. Similarly, the coefficients of variation (CV) for gait
parameters are expected to decline, reflecting greater gait consistency and stability.
Some outcome variables are patient-dependent and require clinical interpretation. For
example, a decrease in cadence may indicate improved gait control in some patients.
However, in individuals with bradykinesia, an increase in cadence may reflect enhanced
motor function. Consequently, interpreting such outcomes without the input of an experi-
enced clinician is challenging.
For the purpose of statistical modeling and mixed-effects analysis, the following variable
annotations are used:
• Outcome: The specific clinical or gait measure analyzed
• Condition: Control (no vibration) vs. Intervention (with vibration)
• Sequence: Order of intervention (CI = Control then Intervention; IC = Intervention
then Control)
• Meeting Number: Session index (1 = baseline, 2 and 3 = follow-up sessions)
• Age: Participant age
• Relative ID: Unique participant identifier
• Time: Duration of insole usage during the assigned condition phase
• Disease Stage: Severity of disease
• Baseline Gait Score: Gait metric at baseline (Meeting 1)
4.2
Participant Demographics and Baseline Characteristics
Participant Flow and Inclusion.
A total of 25 participants were enrolled in the study, including 13 individuals with SCI
and 12 with PD. Of the SCI participants, four were included only in the immediate effect
analysis due to logistical or personal reasons, such as the mental burden of extended
participation. Some declined to take part in the four-week measurement phase for similar
reasons. In total, five participants dropped out of the study for various reasons.
Figures 4.2 and 4.1 present the CONSORT flow diagrams detailing the recruitment, in-
clusion, and dropout processes for both the SCI and PD cohorts.
20
iGRASP
==PAGE:37==
Figure 4.1: CONSORT Flow Diagram for the Parkinson’s Disease Cohort
For the PD cohort, 27 participants were screened: 12 through out-clinic recruitment and
15 via peer mentoring (patient-to-patient contact). Ultimately, 9 participants completed
both the immediate and long-term effect phases, while 1 was included in the immediate
effect analysis only.
Reasons for declining participation included:
• Vacations/travel during the data collection period (2)
• Full-time work, resulting in fatigue or limited availability (5)
• Perceived burden of three visits in a short time frame (2)
• Recent breast cancer diagnosis (1)
Participants were also excluded for the following reasons in the PD cohort:
• No PD diagnosis (1)
• Transportation barriers (3)
One PD participant was excluded from the long-term analysis due to device failure, which
resulted in missing data for visits 2 and 3.
For the SCI cohort, 75 individuals were screened (70 in-patients and 5 recruited through
peer mentoring). In total, 6 participants completed both the immediate and long-term
analyses, and 4 were included in the immediate effect analysis only.
Reasons for declining participation included:
• Lack of time (1)
iGRASP
21
==PAGE:38==
Figure 4.2: CONSORT Flow Diagram for the Spinal Cord Injury Cohort
• Transportation barriers (2)
• Pain (1)
• Discomfort with app installation (1)
• Overload during hospital admission or undisclosed reasons (10)
Participants were also excluded for the following reasons in the SCI cohort:
• Not diagnosed with SCI (2)
• Unable to walk (42)
• Insufficient Danish or English language skills (1)
One SCI participant was excluded from the long-term analysis due non-adherence to the
intervention, thus the intervention to test the long-term effect was not performed. How-
ever, those participants were still included in the immediate effect analysis.
Due to technical issues during data collection with the insoles, fewer participants were
included in the gait characteristic analysis compared to the clinician-reported outcomes.
For instance, in the SCI cohort, although six participants completed the full trial, only three
had complete gait data recorded across all sessions. As a result, gait data can be used
to assess immediate intervention effects, but not long-term effects, since some follow-up
recordings are missing.

Participant Characteristics.
Table 4.2 summarizes the demographic and clinical profiles of SCI and PD participants.
As expected, PD participants were older (68.8 vs. 51.8 years), with similar gender distri-
bution. SCI participants were slightly taller and heavier, with longer diagnosis duration.
Both groups showed moderate baseline impairment: SCI (WISCI: 16.2 ± 7.0), PD (H&Y:
2.3 ± 0.8).

SCI participants showed moderate functional balance (BBS: 43.5 ± 13.4), while PD par-
ticipants demonstrated consistently impaired balance (BESTest: 22.6 ± 3.7), aligned with
increased fall risk. LEDD for PD (600.0 ± 257.1 mg) revealed variability in treatment needs.
A summary table of comorbidities is provided in the Appendix B.2; however, no notable
impact on gait was identified, except for diabetes in participants 1, 17 and 21 (SCI group)
and osteoporosis and scoliosis for participant 7 (PD group), which may have influenced
their outcomes.
Table 4.3 shows gait metrics (mean, asymmetry, CV). PD participants exhibited higher
cadence and stride velocity, reflecting more regular gait. SCI participants had greater
variability and asymmetry, particularly in stride length and swing speed, consistent with
lesion-related motor deficits.
Overall, the data highlight distinct gait profiles in SCI and PD, informing tailored rehabili-
tation strategies.

5
Data Analysis Methods
Figure 5.1 shows the data pre-processing and analysis workflow. Gait and clinical data
were processed to extract features and scores, then cleaned and formatted for analysis.
Linear mixed-effects (LME) models were applied, assumptions checked, and key results
(p-values, effect sizes, power) extracted. All R code is in Appendix C.
Figure 5.1: Workflow for data pre-processing and statistical analysis.
5.1
Data Preprocessing
For the gait parameters, temporal and spatial parameters were extracted as described
previously in Section 3.1. Clinical data, including questionnaire responses and clinician-
conducted gait assessments, were exported from REDCap. All missing values were en-
coded as NA for compatibility with downstream statistical analysis. Final scores were com-
puted following the standardized scoring instructions of each respective instrument [39,
40, 41, 42, 44, 45]. To ensure data robustness, outliers were examined using standard-
ized residuals and leverage statistics.
5.1.1
Processing for Immediate Effect Study
For the immediate effect analysis, the outcome variable X is defined as the within-session
difference between intervention and control conditions (X1b−X1a, X2b−X2a and X3b−X3a
with the annotations from Figure 3.4). Thus, after preprocessing, the resulting table (Ta-
ble 5.1) presents velocity measurements under both Control and Intervention (vibration)
conditions across three time points. It also includes participant ID, age, intervention se-
quence, and WISCI score scores, which indicate the stage of impairment for SCI patients,
similar to the H&Y scale scores used for PD participants.
To assess immediate intervention effects, performance differences between Control and
Intervention conditions were analyzed for each meeting. Only non-missing and non-zero
values were retained. The structure of the dataset used in this analysis is shown in Ta-
ble 5.2.

5.1.3
Dataset Merging Decisions
As a pre-processing step, the feasibility of averaging left and right foot measures, specif-
ically gait statistics (mean and CV variables for each gait parameters) and QST values,
is evaluated. For each feature, the left and right datasets are loaded and merged. The
following methods are applied to assess potential side-related differences:
• Levene’s Test: Assesses the equality of variances between the left and right sides
statistical features. A non-significant p-value (typically p > 0.05) indicates homo-
geneity of variance, supporting the decision to average [51].
• Linear Mixed-Effects Model (LME): Includes Side as a fixed effect and Relative_ID
as a random intercept. A non-significant p-value for the Side term suggests no sys-
tematic side-related differences across individuals.
• Bland–Altman Plots: Provide a visual inspection of agreement between sides. If
differences are small, symmetrically distributed around zero, and independent of
measurement magnitude, averaging is considered valid [52].
For gait data, all Bland–Altman plots for gait parameters are provided in Appendix D.1,
D.2.
Examples of Bland–Altman plots for strike angle are presented in Figures 5.2b
and 5.2a. Visual inspection of the data also appears acceptable, although an increas-
ing trend was observed for some variables. This suggests that a log-transformation of
these gait parameters could be beneficial, a possibility that will be explored later during
the statistical analysis for better perspective.
(a) Bland-Altman Plot for SCI participants.
(b) Bland-Altman Plot for PD participants.
Figure 5.2: Bland–Altman Plot of Left vs. Right Limb Symmetry for the strike angle mean.
A summary of the results from Levene’s test and linear mixed-effects (LME) analyses is
reported in Table 5.4. Except for some specific variables such as cadence mean and
cycle duration mean in the PD cohort, the p-values from Levene’s test are not significant
for both PD and SCI cohorts. This indicates homogeneity of variances and supports the
possibility of averaging right and left side data.
Similarly, when testing for the effect of side, most variables show non-significant p-values,
iGRASP
27
==PAGE:44==
suggesting no significant side effect. Exceptions include variables such as 3D path length,
step length, stride velocity, and swing speed in the PD cohort, as well as 3D path length,
stance, swing, and swing speed in the SCI cohort. Therefore, in the current study, all gait
data are averaged across sides to use a single value per variable, rather than separate
values for right and left sides.

For QST data, all Bland–Altman plots for QST values are provided in Appendix D.3, and
D.4. Log-transformation is necessary to satisfy the assumptions of the Bland–Altman
method. Without transformation, increasing trends are observed (i.e., greater differences
at higher magnitudes), indicating non-constant bias. Consequently, log-transformed QST
values are used throughout the analysis.
A similar approach is applied to evaluate whether data from participants with PD and
SCI can be pooled. Shared gait parameters (e.g., velocity, step length) are compared
between groups using LME models, Levene’s test, and Bland–Altman plots. However,
statistical results (significant p-values in Levene’s test) and clinical considerations indicate
substantial differences in gait characteristics between the two populations. Therefore,
merging data across PD and SCI groups is not pursued in this study.

5.2
Statistical Analysis Methods and Models
The experiment employs a within-subjects (mixed) 2×2 crossover design as explained in
Section 3.4. Each participant experiences both the treatment condition and the control
condition across two periods, allowing individual differences to be controlled by design.
The statistical model includes dependent variables, independent variables, relevant con-
founding factors, and treatment effect parameters. Normality tests, aiming to verify the
normal distribution of specific variables in the model, are first conducted to validate as-
sumptions, followed by hypothesis testing for each outcome measure.
Given the repeated measures structure, a Linear Mixed-Effects (LME) model is employed
[53]. This statistical approach includes both fixed effect and random effect terms. Fixed
effects estimate the average influence of independent variables that are consistent across
all participants, such as treatment condition. In contrast, random effects capture individual-
level variability by allowing model parameters (e.g., intercepts) to differ by participant,
accounting for intra-subject correlation and heterogeneity [54].
In addition to hypothesis testing, simulation is necessary to more accurately assess the
treatment effects, as the variables are not independent. Standard comparison tests, such
as t-tests, would not be appropriate because they assume independence among the vari-
ables, which is not met in a repeated measures design. Simulation methods allow for more
robust conclusions by accounting for the dependence structure among the variables in the
model.
5.2.1
Statistical Hypothesis
The primary objective of this study is to determine whether the intervention condition (vi-
bration feedback via intelligent insoles) produces a statistically significant effect on gait-
related outcomes (e.g., walking velocity, balance measures) compared to the control con-
dition.
The outcome variable Yij represents the gait performance metric (such as walking ve-
locity) recorded for participant i at session j. The binary variable condition indicates
whether the measurement was taken during the intervention phase (condition = 1) or
during the control phase (condition = 0).
The statistical model applied is a Linear Mixed-Effects (LME) model of the following form:
Yij = β0+β1·conditionij+
∑
k
βk·confounderk,ij+
∑
k
γk·(conditionij × confounderk,ij)+ui+εij
Where:
• β0 is the fixed intercept,
• β1 is the fixed effect of the intervention condition,
• βk are fixed effects associated with confounding variables (e.g., age, sequence,
baseline severity),
• γk are the coefficients for interaction terms between condition and each confounder,
• ui ∼N(0, σ2
u) is the random intercept accounting for participant-specific baseline
differences,
• εij ∼N(0, σ2) is the residual error term.
iGRASP
29
==PAGE:46==
Including interaction terms (condition×confounderk) allows the model to capture whether
the effect of the intervention varies across levels of the confounders. For instance, the
intervention might be more effective in younger participants or those with higher baseline
severity. Without these interaction terms, the model would assume that the intervention
has a uniform effect across all participants, regardless of their individual characteristics.
This could lead to misleading conclusions, especially in heterogeneous populations such
as those found in clinical studies.
The random intercept ui accounts for intra-individual correlation due to repeated mea-
sures, ensuring that the estimation properly reflects within-subject changes over time.
The hypotheses tested are:
• Null Hypothesis (H0): β1 = 0, indicating no effect of the intervention on the gait
outcome after adjusting for confounders and interaction terms.
• Alternative Hypothesis (H1): β1 ̸= 0, indicating a significant effect of the interven-
tion on the gait outcome.
For illustration, in PD patients using the Mini-BESTest as the outcome measure:
• H0: β1 = 0, the intervention does not influence Mini-BESTest scores.
• H1: β1 ̸= 0, the intervention significantly changes Mini-BESTest scores.
The model is implemented using the lme() function from the lme4 package.
5.2.2
Confounder Identification and Adjustment
Directed Acyclic Graphs (DAGs) are graphical representations used to illustrate assumed
causal relationships between variables. They are composed of nodes (representing vari-
ables) and directed edges (arrows) that indicate causal influence, under the condition that
the graph contains no closed loops. DAGs are based on theoretical assumptions and prior
domain knowledge; thus, their validity depends on how accurately these assumptions re-
flect reality. While there is no strictly right or wrong way to construct a DAG, its usefulness
lies in its ability to clarify potential sources of bias, guide model specification, and identify
confounders.
In the context of the iGRASP study, the DAG is used to identify and justify the inclusion of
covariates that may confound the relationship between the intervention (use of intelligent
insoles) and gait outcomes. Confounding variables are those that are causally associated
with both the exposure and the outcome. If not properly accounted for, they can bias the
estimation of the treatment effect [55, 56]. The rationale for selecting each confounder
is based on prior knowledge from literature review [57, 58], clinical relevance, and ob-
served correlations with both the predictor and outcome, as justified in the DAG above.
The Directed Acyclic Graph (DAG) shown in Figure 5.3 includes the following potential
confounding variables:
• Age
• Sequence (treatment order in the crossover design)
• Disease stage, including WISCI score and Hoehn & Yahr stage
30
iGRASP
==PAGE:47==
• Baseline Gait Score, including the baseline gait score (BBS for SCI and Mini BESTest
for PD)
• Insole use duration, captured by the variable time
• Meeting number (for short-term measurements)
Figure 5.3: Directed Acyclic Graph of the iGRASP study
In some models, these covariates, referred to as confounders, are included to control for
external factors that might influence the observed relationship between the main predictor
(e.g., condition) and the outcome. This helps reduce bias and isolate the true effect of
the variable of interest. These confounders are incorporated as fixed effects in the linear
mixed-effects (LME) models to adjust for their influence:
yi = β0 + β1 · Xi + β2 · Confounderi + bi + ϵi
Where:
• yi is the outcome (e.g., gait velocity, BBS score),
• Xi is the main independent variable (e.g., condition),
• Confounderi is a vector of confounding covariates,
• bi ∼N(0, σ2
b) is the random effect for participant i,
• ϵi ∼N(0, σ2) is the residual error.
The process of choosing whether to include confounders in the final statistical model in-
volves several steps:
• Statistical assessment: Compare nested models (e.g., with and without a confounder)
using changes in the effect size of the main predictor or p-values.
• Parsimony: Prefer simpler models that adequately fit the data, unless a more com-
plex model significantly improves explanatory power.
• Interpretability: Ensure that the inclusion of confounders does not complicate inter-
pretation or introduce multicollinearity.
iGRASP
31
==PAGE:48==
5.2.3
Model interpretation: p-value and confidence intervals
In LME models, fixed effects estimate the relationship between predictors (e.g., condition
or sequence) and the outcome variable, while accounting for random effects such as
subject-level variability. The interpretation of statistical outputs includes:
• p-values indicate whether an observed effect is statistically distinguishable from
zero, with a threshold typically set at α = 0.05. However, non-significant p-values
may arise not due to a lack of effect but due to insufficient statistical power.
• Confidence intervals (CIs) offer a range within which the true effect is expected to
lie with 95% certainty. A CI including zero suggests non-significance.
• Effect sizes, reflected in the estimated coefficients, describe the magnitude of the
effect. For example, a coefficient of 0.25 for the Condition variable implies a small
average increase in the outcome when the intervention is applied.
5.2.4
Assumptions Verification
LME models are used to account for both fixed and random sources of variation in data,
especially when repeated measures or hierarchical structures are involved. Before inter-
preting model results, several statistical assumptions must be verified to ensure model
validity. These assumptions include:
• Linearity: The outcome should relate linearly to the predictors.
• Normality of Residuals: Residuals (errors) should follow a normal distribution.
• Homoscedasticity: Residuals should have constant variance.
• Independence of Residuals: Residuals should be independent of each other. This
assumption is considered valid for the entire study since participants are indepen-
dent individuals (i.e., no family members or related participants).
• Normality of Random Effects: Random effects should be normally distributed.
Each assumption is checked using specific diagnostic plots and statistical tools, as de-
scribed below.
Random Effects
Random effects capture subject-specific or group-specific deviations from the overall pop-
ulation trend. The typical random effects model is:
yi = β0 + β1 · Xi + bi + ϵi
Where:
• yi is the outcome variable for the i-th observation,
• β0 and β1 are fixed effects (intercept and slope),
• bi ∼N(0, σ2
b) is the random effect for subject or group i,
• ϵi ∼N(0, σ2) is the residual error.
To test the normality assumption for the random effects, a Q-Q (quantile-quantile) plot was
generated. This plot compares the quantiles of the random effects with the quantiles of a
theoretical normal distribution. If the random effects are normally distributed, the points
32
iGRASP
==PAGE:49==
should approximately align with the diagonal line, indicating that the observed distribution
closely follows a normal distribution.
Fixed Effects
Fixed effects represent overall population-level trends. To verify their assumptions, resid-
uals from a model treating participants as fixed rather than random was examined. This
allows us to inspect linearity, normality, and homoscedasticity more directly. Diagnostic
plots were used to check fixed-effects assumptions:
• Residuals vs Fitted: Tests linearity and homoscedasticity; a random scatter sug-
gests assumptions are met.
• Normal Q-Q: Assesses normality of residuals; points should follow the diagonal.
• Scale-Location: Evaluates homoscedasticity; a horizontal spread indicates con-
stant variance.
• Residuals vs Leverage (Cook’s Distance): Detects influential observations; high-
leverage points beyond Cook’s lines may affect model fit.
Example plots are shown in Figure 5.4. Overall, residuals showed reasonable scatter and
normality, with slight curvature and mild tail deviations. Cook’s plot highlighted observa-
tions 31, 63, and 64 as influential, particularly observation 31.
If assumptions are violated, alternative models or transformations should be considered
in future studies.
Figure 5.4: Example of diagnostic plots used to evaluate model assumptions.
Carryover Effect Assessment
In this 2 × 2 crossover design, the potential presence of a carryover effect (i.e., whether
the first-period treatment influences second-period outcomes) is evaluated.
Although
the study assumes negligible carryover (and thus no washout period), this assumption
is tested empirically.
iGRASP
33
==PAGE:50==
Following literature recommendations, the following linear mixed-effects model is fitted
(model rationale detailed in Appendix D.4):
Sumij ∼Sequencei ∗Confoundersi + (1 | Subjectj)
where:
• Sumij is the total outcome for subject j in sequence i (Y1 + Y2),
• Sequencei indicates treatment order (IC or CI),
• Confoundersi includes age, baseline score, or time since injury,
• Sequencei ∗Confoundersi captures both main and interaction effects,
• (1 | Subjectj) accounts for individual variability.
A significant fixed effect of Sequence suggests a difference in total outcomes between IC
and CI groups, indicating a possible carryover effect. However, sequence differences may
also reflect ongoing rehabilitation, particularly in SCI participants, potentially confounding
carryover interpretation.
5.2.5
Model Reduction: Optimum Model
A backward elimination strategy was applied to identify the most parsimonious model
while preserving statistical validity. The process started with a full linear mixed-effects
model including all relevant main effects and interaction terms. Terms were removed step
by step based on their statistical significance.
Interaction terms with the highest p-values were tested first. If an interaction was not sta-
tistically significant and not theoretically justified, it was removed. Once all non-significant
interactions were eliminated, the process continued by evaluating main effects. Main ef-
fects that were not part of any remaining interactions and had high p-values were also
removed.
In cases where there was uncertainty about removing potential confounders, particularly
those with more than two levels, a likelihood ratio test was performed by comparing the
reduced model to the simpler nested model using ANOVA. The associated p, value was
used to assess whether the reduction led to a significant loss of model fit. Additionally,
if multiple variables were found to significantly improve the model, and since only one
model was to be selected for each of the four substudies, it was necessary to identify a
reduced model that adequately accounted for all relevant variables. Therefore, a con-
founder and/or its interaction term was only removed if it was non-significant across all
considered outcome variables; otherwise, it was retained in the model.
This stepwise reduction continued until only statistically significant predictors or terms
essential to the model structure remained. The same procedure was consistently applied
to all outcome variables under analysis.
5.2.6
Power Analysis
Due to the limited sample size in this crossover study, power analysis was conducted using
Monte Carlo simulations. This underlines the importance of evaluating statistical power,
particularly in small-sample studies, to ensure appropriate interpretation of model outputs
and to assess whether the design is sufficiently sensitive to detect clinically meaningful
34
iGRASP
==PAGE:51==
effects. Simulated data were generated based on the estimated parameters from the fitted
LME model:
• α: intercept,
• β1: effect of the intervention (Condition),
• β2: effect of sequence,
• η: standard deviation of the random intercepts (between-subject variability),
• σ: residual error (within-subject variability).
Before full power simulations, a preliminary analysis under the null hypothesis (β1 = 0)
was conducted using Mini BESTest scores, chosen for their stability. The goal was to
estimate how many simulations are needed for the Type I error rate to stabilize at the
nominal 5% level. As shown in Figure 5.5a, the rate converged after around 1,000 itera-
tions. Thus, 1,000 simulations were deemed sufficient for accuracy, but 100 simulations
were used in later analyses to reduce computation time.
(a) Cumulative average of Type I error (p <
0.05) across 10,000 simulations under the null
hypothesis (β1 = 0). The dashed red line indi-
cates the expected 5% level. Stabilization sug-
gests 1,000 simulations are sufficient.
(b) Estimated power for detecting the interven-
tion effect across a range of effect sizes us-
ing 100 simulations for the Mini BESTest score.
The y-axis shows the proportion of p-values <
0.05. The blue line indicates the observed ef-
fect size from the study.
Figure 5.5: Power simulation results. (a) Type I error convergence under the null. (b)
Estimated power across varying effect sizes.
Using this simulation setup, power was then estimated across a range of intervention
effect sizes (β1) (Figure 5.5b). For each effect size, the proportion of simulations that
yielded a p-value < 0.05 for the intervention effect was computed. This power curve
reflects the sensitivity of the current study design in detecting various effect magnitudes.
For instance, if the power to detect an effect of β1 = 0.25 is approximately 12%, there is
a high risk of Type II error (failing to detect a true effect). Therefore, caution is warranted
when interpreting non-significant results, as they may stem from limited sample size rather
than an actual absence of treatment effect.
5.2.7
Step-by-Step Implementation
This section details the methodology followed during the statistical analysis. The same
approach is applied consistently in the presentation of clinical results. Each subsection
iGRASP
35
==PAGE:52==
begins with the presentation of statistical outcomes, including effect sizes and associated
p-values. Ultimately, a single optimal model is selected per substudy (i.e., PD / SCI and
Immediate / Long-term effect) to ensure simplicity and interpretability. The implementation
proceeds through the following steps:
1. Initial model without confounders:
Begin with a simple model excluding confounders to obtain a preliminary overview
of the intervention effects.
2. Full model including all potential confounders:
Incorporate all candidate confounding variables to evaluate their overall impact on
the outcome. From steps 1 and 2, variables showing significant changes in the
intervention effect are selected for further detailed analysis.
3. Assumption checks:
Assess key model assumptions detailed previously. If these assumptions hold here,
they are expected to hold for simpler nested models as well.
4. Model reduction:
Backward elimination is used to simplify the model as detailed previously in section
5.2.5
5. Power analysis:
Conduct a simulation-based power analysis on the reduced model to estimate the
study’s ability to detect true effects.
6. Carryover effect analysis:
For long-term outcomes, test the reduced model for possible carryover effects to
confirm that results are not biased by prior treatment periods.
7. Outlier analysis:
Identify and remove potential outliers to assess their influence on model conclusions
and ensure robustness.
8. Log-transformation check:
Reanalyze key gait variables after log-transformation to determine if this improves
model fit or interpretability.
5.3
Qualitative Data Analysis
As highlighted by Cheng et al. [59], qualitative research helps capture experiential and
contextual factors that quantitative data cannot. In this study, it was essential for assessing
feasibility and gathering participant feedback, which are relevant clinical outcomes.
A structured, manual analysis inspired by NVivo methodology was conducted [60, 61].
The process included:
• Meaning units: Collection of raw feedback.
• Coding: Extraction of relevant keywords [61].
• Sub-categories: Grouping similar codes.
• Themes: Consolidation into broader insights.
Due to limited access to NVivo and the small sample size, coding was done manually.
Feedback was first categorized into: (1) SCI participant feedback, (2) PD participant feed-
36
iGRASP
==PAGE:53==
back, and (3) general feasibility and adverse events. All responses were originally in
Danish and translated into English. Technical issues encountered during the study were
compiled in a separate table.
Finally, a mind map was created to visualize key terms and emerging themes, following
Wong’s recommendations [62].

==PAGE:54==

Clinical Results
This section presents the clinical results related to the effectiveness of the haptic feedback
insoles in PD and SCI participants, assessing both immediate and long-term effects.
Due to the large number of outcomes and diagnostics, this chapter presents key statis-
tically significant and clinically relevant results only. Evolution plots are shown for repre-
sentative variables; full diagnostics and results are in Appendices D.5 and D.6.
6.1
Immediate Effects of Vibrational Haptic Feedback Insoles
on Gait and Balance
This section presents the immediate effects of the vibrational haptic feedback insoles on
gait and balance across both cohorts.
6.1.1
PD Cohort Outcomes
Evaluation of Potential Improvements for the Model Without Confounders
The BESTest score is the only variable to show a statistically significant improvement
following the intervention, as determined by the linear mixed-effects model: p < 0.001,
effect size = 1.829, 95% CI [1.012, 2.645] (see Appendix Table D.2). No other outcome
variables show significant changes.
Visualizations in Figure 6.5 further support the observed improvement.

Both plots demonstrate an increase in BESTest scores during the intervention condition.
In the individual trajectory plot, most participants show improvement while using the in-
soles. A few show no change, and notably, none experienced deterioration. These results
suggest that the vibrational haptic feedback insoles may lead to immediate functional im-
provement in balance for individuals with PD.

Most confounders (age, sequence, H&Y score, and meeting number) do not exhibit an
independent effect on the outcome after adjusting for other variables (β = 0.000, p =

other terms in the model. Several fixed effects show p-values of 1 and estimates of 0 for
multiple confounders, likely because their effects are fully captured within the interaction
terms. The baseline score remains a strong and significant predictor of BESTest outcomes
(β = 1.000, p < 0.001), as expected, confirming that initial performance strongly influences
follow-up measures. Significant interactions include: Intervention × Gender (β = −1.413,
p = 0.003), suggesting smaller improvement in males; and Intervention × Baseline (β =
−0.253, p < 0.001), indicating less gain for those with higher initial Mini BESTest scores,
likely due to a ceiling effect.
Assumptions Verifications
As discussed in Section 5, the validity of the linear mixed-effects models depends on the
fulfillment of certain assumptions. Figure 6.2 presents the evaluation of assumptions for
the random effects. In the Q-Q plot, the random effects follow the red reference line rea-
sonably well, suggesting approximate normality and justifying the assumption of normally
distributed random effects.

Figure 6.2: Quantile-Quantile plot for the distribution of random effects in the PD short-
term analysis without confounders.
Figure 6.3 displays the diagnostics for the fixed effects.
Figure 6.3: Diagnostic plots for verification of fixed effects assumptions in the PD short-
term analysis without confounders.
The fixed effects diagnostic plots show:
• Residuals vs. Fitted: Residuals appear randomly scattered, though a slight curva-
ture and varying spread suggest potential mild non-linearity or heteroscedasticity.
• Normal Q-Q: Residuals largely conform to the expected normal distribution, with
minor deviations at the tails.
• Scale-Location: A mild decrease in variance with higher fitted values indicates
slight heteroscedasticity.
• Cook’s Distance: Cook’s distance highlights observation 15 as moderately influ-
ential, but no extreme leverage points are detected.
Overall, model assumptions are sufficiently met to proceed with interpretation of fixed and
random effects.

==PAGE:58==
Investigation of Outlier (Observation 15)
Observation 15, corresponding to Partici-
pant 3’s Mini BESTest score under the Control condition at Meeting 2, was flagged due
to its relatively high Cook’s distance (0.2). Several factors may explain this influence:
• Non-monotonic evolution: The Mini BESTest scores for Participant 3 under the
Control condition evolve as follows: 19 (Baseline) →18 (Meeting 2) →20 (Meeting
3). This small dip followed by a rebound introduces a mild deviation from a smooth
linear progression, which may result in larger residuals under a linear model.
• Leverage and residual contribution: As Meeting 2 is the middle time point, it
inherently holds more leverage in a linear trajectory. The score of 18 at this time point
slightly diverges from the linear fit estimated from the surrounding values, leading
to a noteworthy residual and an elevated Cook’s distance.
To clarify this trajectory, the following figure and table summarize Participant 3’s Mini
BESTest scores and Total Time Period 2 values across sessions and conditions:

(Control and Intervention).

Selection of the Optimum Model
In this substudy on the immediate effect in the PD cohort, the full modeling process is pre-
sented in detail, as it is the first time such an analysis appears in the document. For the
subsequent substudies, only a summarized version of the process is provided to stream-
line the reporting. The detailed steps of the model reduction strategy are included in
Appendix D.5.1, while a summary of the overall process is shown in Table 6.2.
At each step of the model reduction, non-significant effects are removed.
To decide
whether to exclude the variables ”sequence” or ”meeting number” in successive model
iterations, ANOVA comparisons are conducted between the reduced and previous mod-
els. The resulting p-values are 0.6831 for ”sequence” and 0.5725 for ”meeting number”.
Accordingly, the variable with the higher p-value (i.e., less significant), ”sequence”, is re-
moved first.

The final model includes fixed effects for Intervention, Gender, Baseline Gait Performance,
as well as interaction terms between Intervention and Gender, and between Intervention
and Baseline Gait Performance.
Yij = β0 + β1 · interventionij + β2 · genderij + β3 · baselineGaitij
+ γ1 · (interventionij × genderij) + γ2 · (interventionij × baselineGaitij) + ui + εij
where Yij is the outcome for participant i at measurement j, ui ∼N(0, σ2
u) represents the
participant-level random effect, and εij ∼N(0, σ2) is the residual error term.
An additional ANOVA comparison is performed between the initial model, including all
confounding variables, and the final reduced model. The resulting p-value was 0.8399,
indicating that the reduced model does not significantly differ from the full model. This
suggests that the excluded variables do not contribute meaningfully to explaining the vari-
ability in the data and that the reduced model provides a more parsimonious fit without
compromising explanatory power.
