Final Model Interpretation
The final linear mixed-effects model reveals a statistically
significant and clinically meaningful improvement in Mini-BESTest scores following the in-
tervention (β = 8.968, p < 0.001), indicating enhanced balance function, which is essential
for reducing fall risk and improving mobility in this population.
iGRASP
43
==PAGE:60==
Baseline gait performance is a strong positive predictor of balance outcomes (β = 1.000,
p < 0.001), confirming that individuals with better initial gait tend to maintain better bal-
ance, underscoring the link between gait and balance and the importance of early func-
tional status.
A significant negative interaction between Intervention and Baseline (β = −0.255, p <
0.001) suggests a ceiling effect: those with better baseline gait showed smaller improve-
ments. This indicates the intervention may be especially beneficial for patients starting
with lower gait performance.
Although Gender alone is not a significant predictor (β = 0.000, p = 1.000), its interaction
with Intervention is significant and negative (β = −1.084, p = 0.0105). Given that gender
is coded as 1 for males and 2 for females, this suggests that females showed a reduced
benefit from the intervention compared to males, pointing to possible sex-based differ-
ences in responsiveness and highlighting the potential value of sex-specific rehabilitation
strategies.
Additional Observations:
The reduced model is applied across all clinical and gait parameters to assess intervention
effects. No variables reach statistical significance (p < 0.05) or showed a trend toward
significance (p < 0.1), except for Step Length Asymmetry, detailed below. Results for
this outcome are summarized in Table 6.4, and corresponding boxplots are available in
Appendix D.5.2.

Interpreting asymmetry is challenging, as the ideal value is zero. The intercept estimate
of 31.244 (95% CI: [−23.37, 85.86]) suggests a high baseline asymmetry in the sample.

negative asymmetry, whose clinical relevance is uncertain.

6.1.2
SCI Cohort Outcomes
This section presents the immediate effects of the vibrational haptic feedback insoles on
gait and balance across the SCI cohort.
Evaluation of Potential Improvements for the Model Without Confounders
No significant improvements were observed in any of the variables when using the model
without confounders. However, some gait characteristic variables showed significant im-
provement when confounders were included in the model, as discussed in the next sec-
tion.
Evaluation of Potential Improvements with Full Confounder Model
This subsection presents the results of the statistical analysis incorporating all potential
confounders (Appendix Table D.13). To enhance clarity, only variables for which the inter-
vention effect has a p-value less than 0.5 are included. Among confounders, only those
with a p-value below 0.1 are displayed. For additional insight, Appendix Table D.14 lists
variables where the intervention effect p-value falls between 0.05 and 0.1.
From these results, several key observations emerge:
• Cadence CV, Cycle Duration CV, Stance Asymmetry, Strike Angle Asymmetry, and
Swing CV exhibit statistically significant changes in response to the intervention
(p < 0.05), with a negative intervention effect observed for all CV-related measures.
• Stance CV, Stance Mean, and Swing Mean show non-significant but potentially
meaningful changes (0.05 < p < 0.1), with the intervention associated with a positive
effect on these parameters.
These intervention effects are discussed in more depth following the development of a
reduced model. Based on their statistical relevance, the five significantly affected outcome
variables are selected as input for the dimensionality reduction methodology applied in the
subsequent section.
Verification of Model Assumptions
To evaluate model validity, Q–Q plots for random effects and diagnostic plots for fixed
effects were generated for the full model including all confounders. These visual checks
are presented in Appendix D.6.2.
No substantial deviations from model assumptions were observed for any of the key out-
come models.
Selection of the Optimal Model
In this section, each of the variables identified as potentially significant in the previous
analysis is examined individually. For each variable, a reduced model is constructed by
systematically removing non-significant confounders. The confounders retained in these
individual models are then combined to form a global reduced model for the SCI immediate
effect substudy.
For each outcome, the stepwise removal of confounders is documented, with detailed
tables provided in Appendix D.5.1. The final selected models for each variable are sum-
marized below:
• Cadence CV: The final model includes intervention, sequence, gender, baseline gait
performance, and the interactions between intervention and sequence, intervention
and gender, and intervention with baseline gait performance.
iGRASP
45
==PAGE:62==
• Cycle Duration CV: The final model is identical to that of Cadence CV.
• Stance Asymmetry: As shown in Table D.17, while stance asymmetry was sig-
nificant when considered alone, it became non-significant once covariates were
included. This indicates that the apparent effect of the intervention was actually
explained by differences in confounding variables. Therefore, stance asymmetry
was excluded from further analysis.
• Strike Angle Asymmetry: The final model retains intervention, sequence, base-
line gait performance, WISCI score, and the interactions between intervention and
sequence, intervention and WISCI score, and intervention with baseline gait perfor-
mance.
• Swing CV: The final model includes intervention, baseline gait performance, and
their interaction.
The final model retains variables and interactions included in at least one reduced model.
Age and timepoint were excluded due to non-significance. Retained covariates include
gender, sequence, baseline gait, and WISCI, with their main and interaction effects with
the intervention. The model is defined as:
Yij = β0 + β1 · interventionij + β2 · genderij + β3 · sequenceij + β4 · baselineij
+ β5 · WISCIij + γ1 · (interventionij × genderij) + γ2 · (interventionij × sequenceij)
+ γ3 · (interventionij × baselineij) + γ4 · (interventionij × WISCIij) + ui + εij
(6.1)
The reduced linear mixed-effects model is applied to all gait variables and clinical scores
to uncover potential effects missed by the full model. Final results, including effect sizes,
confidence intervals, and p-values < 0.1, are presented in Table 6.5 and compared with
the full model (Table D.14).
Several gait variables (Cadence_cv, Cycle_Duration_cv, Strike_Angle_asym, and Swing_cv)
remain significant, with lower p-values in the reduced model, suggesting improved sen-
sitivity and fit. Additional significant confounders further clarify interactions between the
intervention, outcomes, and participant characteristics.
Confounders and Biomechanical Alignment
Cadence_cv and Cycle_Duration_cv share
confounders: gender, sequence, and baseline balance (bbs_tot_a), reflecting their shared
focus on temporal gait variability.
Strike_Angle_asym:
In the reduced model, age loses significance, while WISCI-related
effects emerge. The intercept remains significantly above zero (estimate = 255.7, 95%
CI [75.9, 435.5], p = 0.0076), reflecting baseline asymmetry. The intervention reduces
asymmetry (estimate = −380.2, 95% CI [−569.5, −190.9], p < 0.001), indicating improved
gait symmetry.
Emerging Variables: Stance Metrics
Stance_asym is no longer significant in the re-
duced model. Instead, Stance_cv and Stance_mean emerge as significantly influenced
by the intervention and its interactions, offering new insights into postural phase control
during gait.

Gait Instability and Individual Modulation
The intervention consistently increases vari-
ability across several parameters (cadence_cv, cycle_duration_cv, stance_cv, and swing_cv),
indicating greater gait irregularity. For example, cadence variability increases significantly
(estimate = 0.079, p = 0.0139), as does cycle duration (estimate = 0.082, p = 0.0138),
stance (estimate = 0.087, p = 0.0092), and swing (estimate = 0.153, p = 0.0083).
Importantly, these effects are moderated by baseline balance. Higher BBS scores as-
sociate with attenuated increases in variability: cadence_cv (interaction = −0.001, p =
0.0304), stance_cv (p = 0.0172), and swing_cv (p = 0.0095), suggesting that individuals
with better motor control experience fewer destabilizing effects.
Timing Adjustments as a Compensatory Strategy
The intervention shows a trend
toward increased stance duration (estimate = 10.700, p = 0.0687) and decreased swing
duration (estimate = −10.700, p = 0.0687), possibly reflecting a more cautious gait strat-
egy. These changes are also modulated by baseline balance: higher BBS scores reduce
the stance duration increase (interaction = −0.163, p = 0.0861).
It is important to note that changes in stance and swing duration are also patient-dependent
and require clinical interpretation. For instance, an increased stance time may reflect im-
proved stability in some individuals, while in others, particularly those with spasticity or
impaired propulsion, it may indicate compensatory mechanisms or reduced efficiency.
Similarly, a decreased swing time might suggest enhanced gait fluidity in some patients,
but could also reflect limited leg mobility or reduced muscle control in others. As such,
interpreting these timing adjustments without clinical expertise can be challenging, high-
lighting the importance of individualized assessment.
General Remarks and Conclusions
• No learning effect is observed: The ’meeting number’ variable is not included in the
reduced model, indicating that the order of assessment sessions does not signifi-
cantly impact outcomes. This suggests no learning or habituation effect during the
trial.
• Sequence (control/intervention order) does not appear to have a significant effect
overall. Only one interaction between condition and sequence reached significance
for a single variable. This suggests that the order in which participants received
the interventions had minimal impact, supporting the validity of the crossover trial
design. Nonetheless, it would be advisable to re-examine this in future studies to
confirm the robustness of the findings.
• Baseline gait quality strongly predicts outcomes: Baseline performance, especially
balance scores (e.g., BBS), consistently predicts intervention outcomes, reflecting
that initial gait capacity influences how participants respond to treatment.
• Interaction terms are important: The interaction between intervention and baseline
gait performance (e.g., Intervention:bbs_tot_a) frequently reaches significance,
indicating that the intervention’s effect varies with the participant’s initial functional
status. This interaction is generally positive, implying greater benefits for partici-
pants with higher baseline balance.

6.2
Long-term Effects of Vibrational Haptic Feedback Insoles
on Gait and Balance
This section presents the long-term effects of the vibrational haptic feedback insoles on
gait and balance across both cohorts.
6.2.1
PD Cohort Outcomes
Evaluation of Potential Improvements for the Model Without Confounders
The same procedure is repeated once more. First, the statistical outcomes without ad-
justment for confounders are presented in Appendix, Table D.28. None of the variables
show significant changes (no p-values below 0.05), except for UPDRS Part 2 with a size
effect of -3.1 and a p-value of 0.0160.
The evolution of these variables can be observed in closer detail:
(a) Barplot of UPDRS 2 Scores Pre/Post Period
Differences for Control and Intervention Conditions
with Associated p-value
(b) Pairwise UPDRS Part 2 Score Evo-
lution for Both Conditions by Individual
(Color Coded)
Figure 6.5: BESTest Score Evolution for PD Short-Term Analysis Without Confounders
In this analysis, the outcome variable is not the absolute UPDRS Part 2 score, but rather
the difference in score between two timepoints (post - pre), representing individual changes
over each period. A negative difference indicates improvement (i.e., a reduction in symp-
toms).
In the left plot, the average change during the control period is approximately −2, indi-
cating a modest improvement in patients’ UPDRS Part 2 scores between the beginning
and end of the control condition. This likely reflects natural fluctuations in symptoms or
measurement noise unrelated to any intervention effect. During the intervention period,
the average difference is about −4, indicating a larger improvement. Therefore, the treat-
ment is associated with an additional reduction of 2 points in UPDRS Part 2 compared to
control.
This is interpreted as follows:
UPDRSpost −UPDRSpre = −4 ⇒UPDRSpost = UPDRSpre −4
Thus, on average, the intervention led to a 4-point decrease in UPDRS Part 2 score, which
reflects a clinically meaningful improvement in motor symptoms of Parkinson’s disease.

founders, is presented in Table 6.6.

Only the UPDRS Part 2 score shows a significant improvement (p-value=0.0105) in the
unadjusted model (without confounders), while the effect becomes non-significant after
adjustment.
If a variable (e.g., the intervention) is significant in the simple model but not significant in
the adjusted model, as is the case here with a p-value of 0.5031, this typically indicates
that:
• The effect of the intervention is confounded; that is, the association initially observed
may be partly or entirely explained by other variables (e.g., age, baseline status,
gender).
• After controlling for these confounders, the ’pure’ or adjusted effect of the interven-
tion is smaller, more uncertain, or possibly not different from zero.
To investigate this further, the model reduction is performed further.
Assumptions Verifications
The assumptions are not met for the model including all confounders; therefore, the as-
sumptions are verified for the reduced model explored in the following section.
Additionally, carryover effects are assessed (see Appendix Table D.34) in the ’with con-
founders’ model. This analysis tests the effect of sequence (whether participants started
with the control or intervention period) on the summed outcome, as described in the Meth-
ods section. In this table, the effect of sequence is not found to be significant for any
variable, supporting the conclusion that no carryover effects are present. Consequently,
a washout period may not be necessary in future studies.

iGRASP

Several observations can be drawn from the results presented in Table 6.9:
• None of the gait variables reach statistical significance in the current analysis. How-
ever, this does not necessarily imply a lack of effect. It may reflect limited statistical
power due to sample size or variability in measurement.
• Cadence Mean shows a notable trend toward improvement with the intervention,
with an estimated increase of 10.12 steps per minute (p = 0.1008). While this result
does not reach conventional statistical significance, it indicates a potentially mean-
ingful effect that could become significant in a larger sample. Since higher cadence
can also be associated with increased FoG, this finding should be interpreted in the
context of other gait parameters and, ideally, video recordings. However, as step
length does not exhibit any significant change or trend, it suggests that step length
is preserved, supporting the interpretation of a true gait improvement rather than a
compensatory response to FoG.
• The intervention leads to a statistically significant reduction in UPDRS Part 2 Score
(Estimate = −3.100, p = 0.0105), indicating a clinically relevant improvement in ac-
tivities of daily living.
• Gender is also a significant predictor for UPDRS Part 2 Score, with females scoring
on average 2.94 points lower than males (Estimate = −2.937, p = 0.0406), suggest-
ing better perceived non-motor function among women in this cohort.
• Other gait-related parameters, such as Cycle Duration Mean (Estimate = −0.095
seconds, p = 0.1167) and Cycle Duration Asymmetry (gender effect, Estimate =
0.842%, p = 0.1842), show trends toward improvement or group differences, though
these do not reach statistical significance. The lack of significance is likely due to a
combination of small sample size and high inter-individual variability.
• Certain gait variables are not included in the results table because the corresponding
models failed to converge. This likely reflects insufficient data or excessive variability
for those specific measures, preventing reliable parameter estimation.
Model assumptions are assessed for the reduced linear mixed-effects models and are
presented in Figures 6.6 and 6.7.
52

Figure 6.6: Quantile-Quantile (Q-Q) plot assessing the normality of the random effects in
the PD long-term analysis.
Figure 6.7: Diagnostic plots assessing residual distribution and homoscedasticity for fixed
effects in the PD long-term analysis.
The Q-Q plot (Figure 6.6) shows that random effects are approximately normally dis-
tributed, with points closely following the diagonal. Residual plots (Figure 6.7) indicate
homoscedasticity and no major assumption violations, supporting the model’s validity.
Cook’s distance identified two observations (IDs 17 and 18) with moderately elevated
influence ( 0.5):
• Obs. 17: Subject 10, Intervention, Visit 2, UPDRS = 14, Age 75, Change from
baseline = −8.
• Obs. 18: Subject 10, Control, Visit 2, UPDRS = 19, Age 75, Change from baseline
= +2.
Though not extreme (Cook’s distance < 1), these reflect notable response differences
likely due to clinical variability. They were retained in the analysis as clinically plausible
and within influence limits.

==PAGE:70==
Effect of Insole Usage Time on Outcome Changes
Due to the complexity of the full model, time can not be included as a covariate in the main
analysis. Therefore, a separate model including only insole usage time as a confounder
is fitted, and the results are presented in Table D.29.
No p-values for the time variable are found below 0.1, suggesting that insole usage time
does not significantly influence the intervention effect on participants. However, in this
model, none of the intervention p-values are below 0.1 either, including those identified in
the primary analysis.
This indicates that the current model may not be well-suited to capture the effect of the
intervention when accounting for usage time, and further investigation with alternative
modeling approaches is warranted.
6.2.2
SCI Cohort Outcomes
Including multiple confounders causes the degrees of freedom to drop to zero, resulting
in NaN p-values and confidence intervals for the score analysis. For gait analysis, adding
more than one confounder leads to a Singularity in backsolve error, reflecting collinearity
or insufficient variation. Thus, due to the limited sample size, simultaneous inclusion of
all confounders is not feasible; a stepwise approach is used, adding confounders one at
a time to evaluate their individual impact on the intervention effect.
Evaluation of Potential Improvements for the Model Without Confounders
The results presented in this section reflect the estimated effects of the intervention without
adjustment for confounding variables. Outcomes with a p value<0.1 are summarized in

In participants with SCI, most clinical outcomes show no significant improvement with the
intervention. However, SF-36 increases significantly (p = 0.0314; ˆβ = 13.875), suggesting
enhanced perceived quality of life.
For QST, two parameters approach significance:
54
iGRASP
==PAGE:71==
• MDT: ˆβ = −13.376, p = 0.0675
• MPT: ˆβ = 38.748, p = 0.0764
These trends hint at potential somatosensory changes worth exploring in a larger cohort.
Moreover, several gait parameters exhibit statistically significant changes following the
intervention. Notably, 3D Path Length Asymmetry, Step Length Asymmetry (p = 0.0320),
Step Length Mean (p = 0.0015), Stride Velocity Asymmetry (p = 0.0300), and Stride Veloc-
ity Mean (p = 0.0426) were significantly affected. However, contrary to expectations, the
asymmetry parameters showed increased deviation from zero,indicating a deterioration
in gait symmetry. This suggests that the intervention may have acutely disrupted, rather
than improved, spatiotemporal gait regularity in individuals with spinal cord injury.
Interestingly, the reduction in 3D Path Length Mean suggests a more efficient gait tra-
jectory, which typically reflects improvement. These seemingly contradictory outcomes
highlight the complexity of gait responses to intervention and warrant further investiga-
tion. A more in-depth analysis, ideally with a larger and more diverse sample, is needed
to clarify these effects and account for potential inter-individual variability. These findings
are further discussed in the context of visual inspection and user-specific patterns.
Evaluation of Potential Improvements for the Model With Confounders
As explained previously, each confounder is introduced individually to evaluate its direct
effect or its interaction with the intervention on the outcomes. For clarity and conciseness,
only models in which the intervention effect has a p-value below 0.1 are presented.
1. Clinical Score Analysis – Confounder Screening
• Baseline gait performance (bbs_tot_a): Including this as a confounder does not
result in any significant findings (no p-values < 0.1).
• Age: Similarly, no significant influence on intervention effects is observed when ad-
justing for age.

Assumptions Verification
Model assumptions were checked (Appendix D.6.2) and met, supporting model validity.
Table D.35 (Appendix) tests the sequence effect on outcome sums. Significant carryover
is found for QST MDT and QST MPT, which use semi-quantitative discrete scales (e.g.,
64, 128, ..., 1024). Outliers or single misreports can cause large fluctuations.
Large effect sizes (972.369, 1165.071) likely reflect asymmetry in SCI participants, where
one side has near-normal values and the other scores 1024 (complete loss). This in-
flates averages versus less impaired individuals. Sensory perception in SCI varies daily,
causing intra-individual variability. Carryover is tested on sum scores, not differences, so
a sum of 1024 may represent either moderate bilateral impairment or a single extreme
event. Thus, carryover results for QST MDT and QST MPT require cautious interpreta-
tion.
Selection of Optimum Model
Backward selection used in previous substudies is not feasible here due to the small
sample size and limited model complexity. Instead, model selection focuses on key con-
founders associated with outcomes showing intervention effects (p < 0.1). All outcomes
with significant or near-significant intervention effects are examined for confounders (age,
gender, treatment sequence, WISCI score) with p-values below 0.1. These confounders
are then combined into a common, interpretable model.
Table 6.17: Confounder Selection Based on Association with Significant Outcomes
Outcome Variable
Confounders (0.05 < p < 0.1)
Confounders (p < 0.05)

Gender

Swing Speed CV

—
Wisci score, treatment sequence, and gender appear as relevant confounders for multiple
outcomes. These factors are recommended for inclusion in future models, especially in
studies with larger sample sizes, to better control for potential sources of variation in the
intervention effect.
Moreover, QST measures serve as sensitive and objective biomarkers for detecting sub-
tle sensory changes, making them critical target outcomes for evaluating intervention ef-
fects on sensory function. Taken together, the results highlight that QST MPT is par-
ticularly sensitive to the sequence of intervention administration. The strong interaction
with sequence suggests the presence of order effects, possibly due to carryover or learn-
ing across study periods. Improvement in MPT is observed as an increase in mechanical
pain threshold. In contrast, QST VDT demonstrates significant improvement following the
intervention, especially among participants with lower baseline WISCI scores, indicating
greater benefit for individuals with more impaired mobility. This improvement is reflected
by a decrease in VDT and MDT (mechanical detection threshold) values, suggesting en-
hanced sensory perception.

6.2.3
Freezing of Gait for PD Cohort
Although no consistent or clinically relevant effect of the intervention on FoG was observed
during the assessment sessions, trends in FoG frequency during the control and interven-
tion periods can be explored using data from the daily FoG questionnaires. Among the
12 participants with PD, two (IDs 13 and 20) reported experiencing FoG episodes during
the study. Over the 14-day period, the number of FoG episodes was recorded each day.
Tables summarize these data for the control (non-vibration) and intervention (vibration)
periods, respectively.

6.4
Insights from Qualitative Data Analysis
As described in the Methods section, qualitative feedback from participants was collected
and analyzed. The results were visualized using a mind map for each cohort presented
on Figures 6.8 and 6.9. A color code is applied to illustrate positive feedback (green),
negative feedback (red), and neutral feedback (blue). Subcategories are shown with a
grey background, while final categories are displayed in external boxes. The summary
tables compiling qualitative feedback are presented in Tables E.2 and E.3 in Appendix E.
6.4.1
Qualitative Clinical Assessment from Parkinson’s Disease
Participants
The qualitative feedback from PD participants is summarized in Figure 6.8. Below, the
three key themes identified are discussed:
Figure 6.8: Mind map generated from the qualitative analysis performed on the PD cohort.
Device Usability and Tolerability
This main category groups the feedback related to physical discomfort, emotional discom-
fort, physical comfort, and physical pain. Participants reported the insoles as “too rigid”
and sometimes “too thick,” causing compression in shoes and poor fit, especially in nar-
rower footwear. The insoles generated more warmth than standard footwear, which was
perceived positively by some participants, while others found it less comfortable. Limited
size flexibility was an issue due to the availability of only six sizes, leading to fit discom-
fort for some participants. Regarding comfort, one participant described the insoles as
“firmer” but supportive, and more than half of the participants found the vibration feed-
back comfortable while standing or walking.
On the emotional side, noise generated by the vibrations was often described as disturb-
ing, especially in quiet environments, impacting participants’ focus, a critical factor given
PD patients’ susceptibility to freezing of gait and balance issues. This noise also caused
social discomfort or feelings of stigmatization, leading some participants to remove the
insoles in social settings.
62
iGRASP
==PAGE:79==
Although uncommon, pain was reported by one participant (intense cramps) and two oth-
ers who experienced discomfort from removing their regular orthotic insoles.
Sensory Stimulation
This category addresses the vibrational feedback sensations experienced by participants.
Two out of thirteen participants reported a tickling sensation, confirming the presence
of sensory stimulation. One participant described residual somatic perception, such as
lingering vibration sensations after removing the insoles, occasionally persisting into the
night. Interestingly, three out of thirteen participants noted reduced or absent perception
of the vibrations, sometimes asymmetrically between legs. This unexpected observation
highlights the need for further investigation using QST in future studies.
Gait and Balance Awareness
This category includes postural confidence and balance cueing. Postural confidence was
observed by participants themselves, clinicians, and accompanying persons, who noted
improved posture, increased muscle tone, and better stability, particularly during single-
leg stance exercises or when standing on inclined surfaces. Regarding balance cueing,
participants reported an enhanced awareness of pressure distribution on their feet, which
enabled them to react and adjust their posture more effectively. This feedback is encour-
aging for future studies evaluating longer-term use of the insoles.
6.4.2
Qualitative Clinical Assessment from Spinal Cord Injury Participants
The same qualitative analysis was conducted for SCI participants, yielding slightly differ-
ent feedback but with the same three main categories identified (see Figure 6.9).
Figure 6.9: Mind map generated from the qualitative analysis performed on the SCI cohort.
SCI participants also reported physical discomfort, including “uncomfortable vibrations,”
though emotional discomfort was less socially oriented—likely due to their hospitalization
iGRASP
63
==PAGE:80==
and limited social exposure.
Unlike PD participants, the insoles were not described as positively firm, and no residual
somatic sensations were mentioned in the sensory stimulation category.
As for gait and balance awareness, improved postural confidence and balance cueing
were observed, especially during BBS assessments.
6.4.3
Conclusion and Comparison between PD and SCI Participants
Three key themes emerged:
1. Gait and balance awareness: Reported as a positive and expected outcome of
the intervention.
2. Sensory stimulation: Received mixed feedback—some participants felt effects,
others did not or couldn’t detect vibrations.
3. Device usability and tolerability: Most critical for feasibility. Despite potential ben-
efits, participants were unlikely to use the device long-term due to discomfort and
noise. However, vibration feedback was generally well received, suggesting future
improvements should focus on insole design while preserving stimulation.
Positive effects on postural confidence may reflect placebo or short-term exposure but
merit further study through detailed postural analysis.
64
iGRASP
==PAGE:81==
7
Feasibility Results
This section presents the results related to the feasibility of the clinical trial, including trial
design, participant compliance, and data collection methods.
7.1
Participant Compliance with the Medical Device
Insole sensor data enabled extraction of daily activity durations, categorized as sitting,
standing, walking, or other. Since vibration feedback was active only during walking and
standing, these activity types were the focus of subsequent analysis. For each participant,
walking and standing times were summed separately for the Control and Intervention
periods, then normalized by the number of days of actual insole use. This normalization
was necessary due to variability in usage duration caused by logistical constraints.
Figure 7.1 presents average daily usage across the two periods for both PD and SCI
groups. A marked decrease in usage during the Intervention period is observed among
participants with PD, possibly due to discomfort or the audible noise generated by the
vibrating insoles. Participants with SCI also exhibited reduced usage, though the decline
appears less pronounced. Individual-level trends are displayed in Figure 7.2.
(a) Average daily usage for PD partici-
pants.
(b) Average daily usage for SCI partic-
ipants.
Figure 7.1: Mean daily usage (in minutes) of the insoles across Control and Intervention
conditions.
(a) Usage for PD participants.
(b) Usage for SCI participants.
Figure 7.2: Individual average daily insole usage (in minutes) across conditions.
Substantial variability across participants is evident. Close to half of the individuals main-
iGRASP
65
==PAGE:82==
tained consistent use during both conditions. The other half, particularly within the PD
group, reduced their usage by nearly half during the intervention period. This suggests
some challenges with adherence and acceptability of the intervention.
To better assess whether insole usage behavior differed between the Control and Inter-
vention periods, a linear mixed-effects model was fitted:
Usage_per_day ∼Condition + (1|Relative_ID)
This model accounts for repeated measures by including a random intercept for each
participant (Relative_ID). The fixed effect of interest is the Condition, representing the
presence or absence of vibration feedback.
The following hypotheses were formally tested:
• Null hypothesis (H0): No difference in average daily insole usage exists between
the Control and Intervention conditions (βCondition = 0).
• Alternative hypothesis (H1): A difference exists in average daily insole usage
between the two conditions (βCondition ̸= 0).
The linear mixed model results for PD and SCI participants are summarized in Tables
7.1a and 7.1b respectively.
Table 7.1: Linear mixed model results for the Effect of the Condition on the Daily Usage
of Insoles. Significant effects are indicated with * for p < 0.05.

Table 7.2: Summary of linear mixed model results for PD and SCI participants.
For the PD cohort, the model indicated a statistically significant reduction in insole us-
age during the Intervention condition. The estimated effect was −53.0 minutes per day
(p = 0.030), reflecting a moderate to large decrease relative to the residual standard de-
viation (≈35.0 minutes/day). Random effects analysis showed notable between-subject
variability (σ = 54.0), highlighting diverse individual responses to the intervention.
For SCI participants, the model estimated a non-significant reduction of −15.6 minutes
per day (p = 0.154), suggesting a mild trend toward decreased usage without sufficient
evidence for a consistent effect.
66
iGRASP
==PAGE:83==
Conclusion.
The results suggest that vibration feedback may have adversely impacted
adherence among PD participants, potentially due to sensory discomfort or auditory dis-
traction. The pronounced inter-individual variability further emphasizes the importance of
personalizing wearable technologies to enhance user compliance and acceptability.
7.2
Feasibility Evaluation
Data collection challenges, including adverse events and intervention-related disruptions,
are detailed in Appendix E. Key technical issues with the insoles include short battery
life, unstable Bluetooth, app display errors, missing pressure data, and weak or absent
vibration. A full list is available in Table E.1.
Participant feedback is summarized in Table E.4, while Figure 7.3 presents a mind map
of feasibility insights from participants, clinicians, and investigators. Six themes emerge:
participants, intervention, study design, data analysis, recruitment, and device, covering
both strengths and limitations. These are discussed in the next section.
Figure 7.3: Mind map generated from the feasibility analysis on both PD and SCI cohorts.
Intervention Feasibility
Intervention effectiveness may depend on insole usage patterns—both daily duration and
distribution. Future work should define the minimum effective use and assess how vari-
ability affects outcomes. In this study, compliance was good, with usage ranging from 1
to 10 hours daily (Section 7.1).
Most participants found the insoles user-friendly, with simple charging and minimal main-
tenance. However, unclear charging indicators and insufficient battery life—especially
with active vibration—were noted during longer sessions.
iGRASP
67
==PAGE:84==
Technical issues included unstable vibration feedback and frequent Bluetooth disconnec-
tions, disrupting both stimulation and data collection. Variability in motor strength and
poor vibration localization may also impact effectiveness.
Study Design Considerations
Gait performance variability poses a major challenge, especially in PD and SCI partic-
ipants.
Medication timing (e.g., Levodopa) and rehabilitation fatigue influence perfor-
mance and may mask intervention effects. The 30-minute gait tests often induce fatigue
with better early performance and declines later regardless of intervention.
Lack of blinding introduces potential bias since participants and investigators know inter-
vention conditions. Gait tests may lack sensitivity to detect subtle improvements. Subjec-
tive participant feedback often provides more meaningful insights than objective metrics.
Continuous and real-world assessment approaches could improve outcome evaluation.
Instructions to clinicians are clear and well-understood, supporting reliable protocol im-
plementation, an essential feasibility factor.
Recruitment
Session durations of 1–1.5 hours are manageable for both clinicians and participants.
However, recruitment is challenging, as the trial mostly attracts individuals with mild im-
pairments, those with severe limitations often cannot commit to the 2–4 week protocol.
Frequent rescheduling due to illness, falls, or adverse events adds delays. Targeting the
right population is difficult: some are too impaired to benefit, while others show minimal
room for improvement. The short trial duration further limits enrollment and statistical
power.
Statistical and Data Analysis Considerations
Data management (acquisition, organization, and extraction) is generally strong. Statisti-
cal limitations arise from small sample size and high data variability, due to both technical
inconsistencies and natural participant fluctuations (daily performance, disease progres-
sion).
Excluding participants with maximum baseline balance scores may be necessary, as they
lack scope for improvement and could skew results. Analyzing affected versus unaffected
side rather than left versus right leg better reflects clinical realities.
Device: Data Collection and Technical Challenges
Significant data loss occurs from unreliable Bluetooth connections, especially during the
10MWT. Multiple participants experience disconnections, causing gaps in intervention and
control data. Additional issues include failed charging, weakening vibrations, ongoing
connectivity problems, failure to transmit daily activity data, and hardware malfunctions
such as pressure sensor failures leading to complete data loss.
Limited hardware adaptability restricts insoles to six sizes, affecting fit for all shoe types
and foot anatomies.
Inconsistent vibration intensity from motor fatigue, poor connections, or charging variabil-
ity causes non-uniform intervention experiences, undermining trial standardization and
complicating result interpretation.
68
iGRASP
==PAGE:85==
Participants
Participant compliance poses challenges. Several report discomfort or frustration from
changing shoes. Two-thirds find vibration motor noise disturbing, negatively affecting
adherence. These usability issues likely reduce intervention consistency and influence
outcomes.
Positive Feasibility Aspects of the Study
Strong planning and coordination among researchers, clinicians, and participants foster
efficient collaboration and clear communication.
Participant engagement remains high; many express enthusiasm and curiosity. Posi-
tive subjective effects on gait are often reported even when objective metrics show little
change. The application interface, instructions for use, and guidance materials are gen-
erally clear and easy to follow, supporting adherence.
Test procedures reduce cognitive and physical fatigue, with appropriate session durations.
Substantial walking data collection establishes a strong foundation for future pilot and full-
scale studies.
iGRASP
69
==PAGE:86==
70
iGRASP
==PAGE:87==
8
Discussion and Conclusion
This section concludes the iGRASP project by first evaluating the statistical power of the
conducted experiments, then assessing whether the targeted objectives were met, and
finally discussing potential improvements and future directions.
8.1
Study Evaluation: Statistical Power and Sample Size
Considerations
Statistical power, the probability of detecting a true effect, may be low due to high intra-
subject variability or limited sample size. In such cases, a non-significant p-value does not
necessarily indicate the absence of an intervention effect but may reflect insufficient sen-
sitivity to detect it. This section supports interpretation of non-significant findings across
several outcomes. Only reduced models are assessed, as they represent final analyses.
In the PD immediate effect analysis (Table D.27), Mini BESTest shows high power (0.99),
while most gait metrics remain underpowered (e.g., velocity: 0.13). Asymmetry and vari-
ability measures generally fall below 0.20.
In the SCI immediate analysis (Appendix Table D.26), all models remain below 29%
power, consistent with the limited number of participants.
For PD long-term effects (Appendix Table D.32), most outcomes show power below 41%,
except UPDRS Part 2, which reaches 74% and achieves significance.
In the SCI long-term analysis, several gait metrics (e.g., Path and Step Length Asymmetry
and Mean as well as Stride Velocity Asymmetry) show both significant effects and power
above 80%. SF-36 reaches 74%; other outcomes range from 1–55% power.
All outcomes with sufficient power show significant effects, increasing confidence in their
reliability. Conversely, the lack of significant results in underpowered outcomes calls for
cautious interpretation and highlights the need for larger future samples.
8.2
Results Discussion
First and foremost, it is important to emphasize once again that all these results show
considerable variability. This is expected, as the LME model is a predictive model with low
statistical power in this context. For more reliable and consistent results, cross-validation
techniques could be considered in future work. Table 8.1 summarizes the findings from
the iGRASP study.
iGRASP
71
==PAGE:88==
Objective-Based Interpretation of Results
Figure 8.1 addresses the primary and secondary objectives outlined in Section 1.3.
Figure 8.1: Summary of Statistical Results from the iGRASP Study
Primary Objectives
Despite a limited number of participants, significant results were observed for both imme-
diate and long-term effects in the PD and SCI cohorts, which is encouraging.
• For PD patients:
– Immediate effects: A significant improvement in balance was observed, par-
ticularly through the MiniBESTest, with a mean increase of 8.9 points (out of
28), indicating a strong functional response. Baseline gait performance and
gender (both individually and in interaction) appeared to influence participants’
responsiveness to the intervention due to a potential ceiling effect.
– Long-term effects: A mean decrease of 3 points in UPDRS Part II scores sug-
gests improved motor function in daily life. Only gender showed a significant
main effect on the outcome, with no significant interaction effects identified.
• For SCI patients:
– Immediate effects: An increase in gait variability (e.g., coefficient of varia-
tion) was noted, indicating some destabilization. However, improvements were
seen in asymmetry measures such as strike angle. Baseline gait performance
was consistently associated with the outcome, both as a main effect and through
interactions, similarly to the PD group.
– Long-term effects: Although the sample size was limited, trends indicated im-
provements in walking velocity (e.g., velocity, stride speed, swing speed) and
balance (e.g., BBS), despite a potential increase in asymmetry. Gender and
WISCI scores significantly influenced the outcomes, both through main and in-
teraction effects. Additionally, the sequence of conditions may have introduced
72
iGRASP
==PAGE:89==
a learning effect, particularly visible in QST performance.
Secondary Objectives
• Quality of Life: No significant change was detected in PDQ-39 scores but SF-36
scores showed significant improvement. Although, this was based on a simplistic
model and requires confirmation in future studies.
• Effect of Vibration Usage: No relationship was found between the amount of daily
use and the magnitude of improvements. Further studies with better tracking meth-
ods are needed to assess this.
SCI participants appeared to exhibit more gait-related changes compared to PD partici-
pants. However, the relatively short duration of the intervention (7–14 days) may not be
sufficient to observe stable long-term effects. As participants were evaluated without vi-
bration at each session, potential benefits from wearing the insoles may have been lost
once they were removed. Future studies should consider evaluating participants while
wearing the insoles, or extend the observation period to better assess lasting changes.
Moreover, gender frequently emerged as a confounding factor, with interaction effects
suggesting a protective role; women appeared less sensitive to the intervention.
Feasibility Objectives
• Feasibility of Implementation: The study setup ran smoothly with strong collab-
oration between teams. Some participants reported discomfort or noise from the
insoles, suggesting a need for product refinement or better onboarding. Technical
issues early in the trial caused data loss and increased burden. A newer insole
version improved reliability but still required close monitoring.
• Evaluation of Tools and Methods: MiniBESTest and BBS effectively captured bal-
ance changes and were well-received by clinicians. Notably, the MiniBESTest is
specifically developed for Parkinson’s disease (PD) populations, making it a widely
used and validated tool, a gold standard for balance assessment. Future assess-
ments may focus on the “Sensory Orientation” domain, excluding “Reactive Postural
Control.”
Quality of life questionnaires (PDQ-39, SF-36) were lengthy and inconsistent; shorter
versions may be more effective.
QST showed potential for SCI patients but requires longer follow-up and clearer
interpretation, as results depend on baseline sensitivity. A composite score could
support interpretation with less clinician input. The asymmetry index used was hard
to interpret; using the absolute difference in the ratio would be more intuitive.
• Data Collection and Analysis: The study power was low, often under 20%, due to
small sample size and high variability. This may stem from insole-based measures
and inconsistent 10MWT performance. Additional gait trials could reduce variability
and data loss.
Other Potential Analyses
Raw sensor data enable time-series analysis to extract variability, entropy, or stability
metrics, while machine learning methods could classify gait patterns or predict responses
using multi-sensor inputs.
iGRASP
73
==PAGE:90==
Additionally, the data support power simulations and sample size estimates to inform the
design of future, adequately powered studies.
8.3
Strengths and Limitations of the Study
Limitations
This study assessed the feasibility of pressure-sensitive insoles with vibration feedback
to improve gait in individuals with PD and SCI. While the intervention showed promise,
several limitations affected its impact.
Technical issues, such as unstable Bluetooth connections, inconsistent vibration inten-
sities, and battery failures, led to data loss and protocol deviations. Participants also
reported discomfort from vibration noise and insole fit, impacting adherence.
External factors like Levodopa fluctuations and fatigue further complicated result inter-
pretation, as did the absence of blinding and variability in gait assessments. High within-
subject variability and reliance on subjective outcomes (e.g., UPDRS, questionnaires)
made it difficult to isolate intervention effects from natural fluctuations.
Recruitment was limited to mildly impaired participants due to the protocol’s demands.
This, combined with the short study duration, restricted both sample size and generaliz-
ability, reducing statistical power.
Moreover, while some statistically significant changes were observed in gait parameters,
their clinical relevance is not always straightforward. Such changes can be difficult to
interpret in isolation and require clinician insight or visual assessment to understand the
underlying phenomena. For example, a change in cadence may be ambiguous unless
complemented with additional parameters such as step length or stride time, which can
help contextualize gait adaptations more accurately.
8.4
Future Perspectives
Improving device reliability, ensuring stable Bluetooth, longer battery life, and consistent
motor output, is essential. Optimizing vibration placement may also enhance feedback
effectiveness.
Future protocols should test alternative intervention durations and include adapted ver-
sions for participants with more severe impairments. Identifying specific BESTest subsec-
tions responsive to vibration could clarify functional impact. Real-world gait monitoring
may also reveal intervention effects under daily-life conditions.
Refinements in participant selection and blinding are crucial. Tighter inclusion criteria and
blinded study design would reduce bias. Addressing data loss and excluding participants
with maximum baseline scores would improve data quality. Comparing affected and un-
affected sides may also yield additional insights.
To improve power and generalizability, larger sample sizes, achieved by extending re-
cruitment or expanding to multiple sites, are necessary. While this study employed lin-
ear mixed-effects models to evaluate immediate and long-term effects, future work could
benefit from Bayesian hierarchical models. These approaches offer robustness in small-
sample settings, better uncertainty handling, and improved modeling of missing data and
complex random structures.
Addressing these aspects will support more reliable evaluations of vibration-based insoles
and their potential for gait rehabilitation in PD and SCI populations.
74
iGRASP
==PAGE:91==
8.5
Conclusion
This feasibility study demonstrated improved balance in the PD cohort and increased
gait velocity in the SCI cohort, although some asymmetrical gait patterns were observed.
Overall, the findings highlight the value of feasibility trials in clinical research. Such studies
are essential for refining study design, assessing device usability, and ensuring that data
collection tools are both relevant and reliable for the target population.
Despite the small sample size, some statistically and clinically meaningful effects were
observed, especially in gait and balance outcomes for both Parkinson’s disease and spinal
cord injury groups.
The study also highlights the complex but valuable intersection between statistical sig-
nificance and clinical relevance. Interpreting results through both lenses is essential for
guiding future research.
Overall, the iGRASP study provides a solid foundation for larger trials and points to several
areas for methodological improvement and deeper investigation.

Gait Parameters and Analysis
Figure A.2: Model of gait in individuals with Parkinson’s disease (PD), extending prior

iGRASP
==PAGE:101==
A.3
Gait events description
Feature
Definition
Heel Strike
The moment when the heel first contacts the ground, marking the
beginning of the gait cycle.
Foot Flat
The point at which the entire foot is in contact with the ground,
following heel strike.
Heel Rise
The point at which the heel lifts off the ground as the body transi-
tions forward.
Toe Off
The moment when the toes push off the ground, marking the end
of stance phase and the beginning of the swing phase.
Loading Response
The period immediately after heel strike when weight is trans-
ferred onto the leading foot.
Mid-Stance
The phase where the body’s center of gravity is directly over the
foot in contact with the ground.
Terminal Stance
The final phase of single-limb support, ending with heel rise and
preparing for toe-off.
Pre-Swing
The transition period from stance to swing, where weight shifts to
the opposite limb.
Initial Swing
The early phase of limb advancement, beginning just after toe-off.
Mid-Swing
The period when the swinging limb moves forward and aligns with
the stance limb.
Terminal Swing
The final phase of the swing period, where the limb decelerates
in preparation for the next heel strike.
Initial Double Support
The short period where both feet are in contact with the ground
at the beginning of the stance phase.
Initial Single Support
The phase where only one foot supports the body, following the
first double support phase.
Second Double Support
Another brief period when both feet are in contact with the ground,
before toe-off of the stance foot.
Second Single Support
The phase where the opposite foot supports the body after the
second double support phase.
Stance Phase
The period when the foot is in contact with the ground, covering
heel strike to toe-off.
Swing Phase
The period when the foot is off the ground, allowing it to move
forward for the next step.
Table A.1: Key Features of the Gait Cycle and Their Definitions [3][18].

B.3
Detailed Inclusion and Exclusion Criteria
Inclusion Criteria for Parkinson’s Disease Participants:
• Diagnosis of Parkinson’s disease
• Age over 18 years
• Experiencing motor symptoms such as balance, gait, or postural disturbances, in-
cluding Freezing of Gait (FoG)
• Ability to travel to the hospital independently or with assistance from a spouse or
caregiver
• Willingness to wear intelligent insoles with slight vibrations
• Ability to understand the participant information document and communicate in En-
glish or Danish
Inclusion Criteria for Spinal Cord Injury Participants:
• Diagnosis of incomplete Spinal Cord Injury
• Age over 18 years
• Ability to walk independently for at least 10 meters or with personal assistance
• Experiencing motor symptoms such as balance, gait, or postural disturbances or
sensory symptoms
• Ability to travel to the hospital independently or willingness to participate during hos-
pital admission
• Willingness to wear intelligent insoles with slight vibrations
• Ability to understand the participant information document and communicate in En-
glish or Danish
Exclusion Criteria:
• Cognitive impairment or dementia interfering with survey procedures and data col-
lection
• Lack of a smartphone capable of downloading the Walk With Path app
88
iGRASP
==PAGE:105==
• Inability to understand or communicate in Danish or English
• Presence of other neurological or musculoskeletal disorders significantly impairing
motor function
• Shoe size outside the range of 37 to 48
• History of significant orthopedic injuries or surgeries affecting walking or mobility, as
determined by the project manager
• Pregnancy or breastfeeding
• Presence of a pacemaker
• Any other safety concerns identified by the project coordinator
B.4
Gait and Balance Evaluation Methods
B.4.1
Assessments for Parkinson’s Disease
For PD, the selected assessments were:
• UPDRS II and III [39] – Completed by the clinician/investigator
The Unified Parkinson’s Disease Rating Scale is a validated tool for evaluating PD
symptoms, treatment effects, and progression. The template can be found in the
Appendix B.4.2.
Part II assesses daily activities (e.g. speech, hygiene, and walking; score range:
0–52).
Part III focuses on motor symptoms (e.g. tremor, rigidity, and gait; score range:
0–132). Only items 7–14 were analyzed because others were less relevant. Lower
scores indicate better health.
• PDQ-39 [40] – Completed by the participant
This validated questionnaire evaluates quality of life in PwP and is widely used in
clinical research. To reduce burden from repeated completion, only items 1–10 (Mo-
bility) and 37–39 (Bodily Discomfort) were analyzed. Lower scores indicate better
perceived health.
Each dimension score was calculated as:
Dimension Score =
(
sum of item scores
maximum possible score
)
× 100
The PDQ-39 subscore index (SI) was the average of these two dimension scores.
• Mini BESTest [41] – Completed by the clinician/investigator
This test assesses balance across four domains: anticipatory and reactive postural
control, sensory orientation, and dynamic gait. Each of the 14 items is scored 0–2;
higher scores reflect better balance. It takes 15–20 minutes and helps predict fall
risk. The template can be found in Appendix B.4.2. The total and subscores were
compared across conditions.
• Number of FoG per Session – Recorded by the clinician during assessments.
• Daily FoG Questionnaire – Completed by the participant
Participants recorded daily FoG episodes and shared feedback on their health and
the insoles, this way helping to track changes over time.
iGRASP
89
==PAGE:106==
B.4.2
Assessments for Spinal Cord Injury
For SCI, the assessments included:
• BBS [42] – Completed by the clinician/investigator
The BBS evaluates balance and functional mobility through 14 tasks (e.g. standing
and turning), each scored 0–4 (total: /56). It is widely used in neurological popula-
tions. Higher scores indicate better function. The template is attached in Appendix
B.4.2.
• QST (MPT, MDT, VDT) [42, 43, 44] – Completed by the clinician/investigator
QST assesses sensory thresholds relevant to mechanoreception and vibration. These
are the areas most likely influenced by the insoles.
MPT was tested using pinprick stimulators, MDT with Von Frey filaments, and VDT
using a tuning fork (Figure B.1).
(a) Pinprick (MPT) and tuning fork (VDT).
(b) Von Frey filaments (MDT).
Figure B.1: QST tools used for SCI participants.
For MPT/MDT, five ”yes”/”no” responses per foot were used to calculate geometric
means. VDT was assessed three times per foot and recorded when vibration was
no longer perceived. Six outcomes were analyzed: MDT-right/left, MPT-right/left,
and VDT-right/left.
• SF-36 [45] – Completed by the participant
This questionnaire assesses health-related quality of life via 36 items across phys-
ical and mental domains. Common in SCI research, it helps monitor health and
treatment impact. The total score and three dimensions: physical functioning, role
limitations (physical), and bodily pain were analyzed. Higher scores indicate better
health.

Mini-BESTest: Balance Evaluation Systems Test

_________________________________________________________________________________________________ 
1. SIT TO STAND 
Instruction: “Cross your arms across your chest. Try not to use your hands unless you must.  Do not let your legs lean 
against the back of the chair when you stand. Please stand up now.” 
(2) Normal: Comes to stand without use of hands and stabilizes independently. 
(1) Moderate: Comes to stand WITH use of hands on first attempt. 
(0) Severe: Unable to stand up from chair without assistance, OR needs several attempts with use of hands. 
 
2. RISE TO TOES 
Instruction: “Place your feet shoulder width apart. Place your hands on your hips. Try to rise as high as you can onto your 
toes. I will count out loud to 3 seconds. Try to hold this pose for at least 3 seconds. Look straight ahead. Rise now.” 
(2)  Normal:  Stable for 3 s with maximum height.  
(1)  Moderate: Heels up, but not full range (smaller than when holding hands), OR noticeable instability for 3 s. 
(0)  Severe:  < 3 s. 
 
3. STAND ON ONE LEG  
Instruction: “Look straight ahead. Keep your hands on your hips. Lift your leg off of the ground behind you without touching or 
resting your raised leg upon your other standing leg. Stay standing on one leg as long as you can. Look straight ahead. Lift 
now.” 
Left:   Time in Seconds Trial 1:_____Trial 2:_____ 
(2) Normal: 20 s. 
 
(1) Moderate: < 20 s. 
 
(0) Severe: Unable. 
Right:   Time in Seconds Trial 1:_____Trial 2:_____ 
(2) Normal: 20 s. 
 
 
 
(1) Moderate: < 20 s. 
 
(0) Severe: Unable 
To score each side separately use the trial with the longest time.  
To calculate the sub-score and total score use the side [left or right] with the lowest numerical score [i.e. the worse side].   
 
_________________________________________________________________________________________________ 
4. COMPENSATORY STEPPING CORRECTION- FORWARD 
Instruction: “Stand with your feet shoulder width apart, arms at your sides.  Lean forward against my hands beyond your 
forward limits. When I let go, do whatever is necessary, including taking a step, to avoid a fall.” 
(2)    Normal:  Recovers independently with a single, large step (second realignment step is allowed). 
(1)    Moderate: More than one step used to recover equilibrium.  
(0)    Severe:  No step, OR would fall if not caught, OR falls spontaneously. 
 
5. COMPENSATORY STEPPING CORRECTION- BACKWARD 
Instruction: “Stand with your feet shoulder width apart, arms at your sides. Lean backward against my hands beyond your 
backward limits. When I let go, do whatever is necessary, including taking a step, to avoid a fall.” 
(2)    Normal: Recovers independently with a single, large step. 
(1)    Moderate: More than one step used to recover equilibrium. 
(0)    Severe: No step, OR would fall if not caught, OR falls spontaneously. 
 
6. COMPENSATORY STEPPING CORRECTION- LATERAL 
Instruction: “Stand with your feet together, arms down at your sides. Lean into my hand beyond your sideways limit. When I 
let go, do whatever is necessary, including taking a step, to avoid a fall.” 
Left  
(2)  Normal: Recovers independently with 1 step 
(crossover or lateral OK). 
(1)  Moderate: Several steps to recover equilibrium. 
(0)   Severe: Falls, or cannot step. 
Right 
(2)  Normal: Recovers independently with 1 step 
(crossover or lateral OK). 
(1)  Moderate: Several steps to recover equilibrium. 
(0)   Severe:  Falls, or cannot step. 
Use the side with the lowest score to calculate sub-score and total score.

shoulder width apart and have your arms down at your sides. I will start timing when you close your eyes.”

____________________________________________________________________________________________________ 
10. CHANGE IN GAIT SPEED  
Instruction: “Begin walking at your normal speed, when I tell you ‘fast’, walk as fast as you can. When I say ‘slow’, walk very 
slowly.” 
(2) Normal: Significantly changes walking speed without imbalance. 
(1) Moderate: Unable to change walking speed or signs of imbalance. 
(0) Severe: Unable to achieve significant change in walking speed AND signs of imbalance. 
 
11. WALK WITH HEAD TURNS – HORIZONTAL  
Instruction: “Begin walking at your normal speed, when I say “right”, turn your head and look to the right.  When I say “left” 
turn your head and look to the left.  Try to keep yourself walking in a straight line.” 
(2)  Normal: performs head turns with no change in gait speed and good balance. 
(1)  Moderate: performs head turns with reduction in gait speed.  
(0)  Severe: performs head turns with imbalance. 
 
12. WALK WITH PIVOT TURNS 
Instruction: “Begin walking at your normal speed. When I tell you to ‘turn and stop’, turn as quickly as you can, face the 
opposite direction, and stop. After the turn, your feet should be close together.” 
 
 
 
 
 
 
(2) Normal: Turns with feet close FAST (< 3 steps) with good balance. 
(1) Moderate: Turns with feet close SLOW (>4 steps) with good balance. 
(0) Severe: Cannot turn with feet close at any speed without imbalance. 
 
13. STEP OVER OBSTACLES  
Instruction: “Begin walking at your normal speed. When you get to the box, step over it, not around it and keep walking.” 
 
(2)  Normal: Able to step over box with minimal change of gait speed and with good balance. 
(1)  Moderate: Steps over box but touches box OR displays cautious behavior by slowing gait. 
(0)  Severe: Unable to step over box OR steps around box. 
 
14. TIMED UP & GO WITH DUAL TASK [3 METER WALK] 
Instruction TUG: “When I say ‘Go’, stand up from chair, walk at your normal speed across the tape on the floor, turn around, 
and come back to sit in the chair.”  
Instruction TUG with Dual Task: “Count backwards by threes starting at ___. When I say ‘Go’, stand up from chair, walk at 
your normal speed across the tape on the floor, turn around, and come back to sit in the chair. Continue counting backwards 
the entire time.”  
      TUG: ________seconds; Dual Task TUG: ________seconds 
(2) Normal: No noticeable change in sitting, standing or walking while backward counting when compared to TUG without 
Dual Task.  
(1) Moderate: Dual Task affects either counting OR walking (>10%) when compared to the TUG without Dual Task.  
(0) Severe: Stops counting while walking OR stops walking while counting.  
When scoring item 14, if subject’s gait speed slows more than 10% between the TUG without and with a Dual Task the score 
should be decreased by a point.

==PAGE:109==
 
Mini-BESTest Instructions 
Subject Conditions: Subject should be tested with flat-heeled shoes OR shoes and socks off. 
Equipment: Temper® foam (also called T-foamTM 4 inches thick, medium density T41 firmness rating), chair without arm rests or wheels, 
incline ramp, stopwatch, a box (9” height) and a 3 meter distance measured out and marked on the floor with tape [from chair]. 
Scoring: The test has a maximum score of 28 points from 14 items that are each scored from 0-2.   
“0” indicates the lowest level of function and “2” the highest level of function.  
If a subject must use an assistive device for an item, score that item one category lower.  
If a subject requires physical assistance to perform an item, score “0” for that item.  
For Item 3 (stand on one leg) and Item 6 (compensatory stepping-lateral) only include the score for one side (the worse score).   
For Item 3 (stand on one leg) select the best time of the 2 trials [from a given side] for the score. 
For Item 14 (timed up & go with dual task) if a person’s gait slows greater than 10% between the TUG without and with a dual task then the 
score should be decreased by a point.  
 
1. SIT TO STAND 
Note the initiation of the movement, and the use of the subject’s hands on the seat of the chair, the 
thighs, or the thrusting of the arms forward. 
2. RISE TO TOES 
 
Allow the subject two attempts. Score the best attempt. (If you suspect that subject is using less than full 
height, ask the subject to rise up while holding the examiners’ hands.)  Make sure the subject looks at a 
non-moving target 4-12 feet away. 
3. STAND ON ONE LEG 
 
Allow the subject two attempts and record the times. Record the number of seconds the subject can hold 
up to a maximum of 20 seconds.  Stop timing when the subject moves hands off of hips or puts a foot 
down. Make sure the subject looks at a non-moving target 4-12 feet ahead. Repeat on other side. 
4. COMPENSATORY STEPPING 
CORRECTION-FORWARD 
Stand in front of the subject with one hand on each shoulder and ask the subject to lean forward (Make 
sure there is room for them to step forward). Require the subject to lean until the subject’s shoulders and 
hips are in front of toes. After you feel the subject’s body weight in your hands, very suddenly release 
your support. The test must elicit a step. NOTE: Be prepared to catch subject. 
5. COMPENSATORY STEPPING 
CORRECTION - BACKWARD 
Stand behind the subject with one hand on each scapula and ask the subject to lean backward (Make 
sure there is room for the subject to step backward.) Require the subject to lean until their shoulders and 
hips are in back of their heels. After you feel the subject’s body weight in your hands, very suddenly 
release your support. Test must elicit a step.  NOTE: Be prepared to catch subject. 
6. COMPENSATORY STEPPING 
CORRECTION- LATERAL 
Stand to the side of the subject, place one hand on the side of the subject’s pelvis, and have the subject 
lean their whole body into your hands. Require the subject to lean until the midline of the pelvis is over 
the right (or left) foot and then suddenly release your hold. NOTE: Be prepared to catch subject. 
7. STANCE (FEET TOGETHER); 
EYES OPEN, FIRM SURFACE 
Record the time the subject was able to stand with feet together up to a maximum of 30 seconds.  Make 
sure subject looks at a non-moving target 4-12 feet away. 
8. STANCE (FEET TOGETHER); 
EYES CLOSED, FOAM SURFACE 
Use medium density Temper® foam, 4 inches thick.  Assist subject in stepping onto foam. Record the 
time the subject was able to stand in each condition to a maximum of 30 seconds. Have the subject step 
off of the foam between trials. Flip the foam over between each trial to ensure the foam has retained its 
shape. 
9. INCLINE EYES CLOSED 
Aid the subject onto the ramp. Once the subject closes eyes, begin timing and record time. Note if there 
is excessive sway. 
10. CHANGE IN SPEED 
Allow the subject to take 3-5 steps at normal speed, and then say “fast”. After 3-5 fast steps, say “slow”.  
Allow 3-5 slow steps before the subject stops walking. 
11. WALK WITH HEAD TURNS- 
HORIZONTAL 
Allow the subject to reach normal speed, and give the commands “right, left” every 3-5 steps.  Score if 
you see a problem in either direction. If subject has severe cervical restrictions allow combined head and 
trunk movements. 
12. WALK WITH PIVOT TURNS 
Demonstrate a pivot turn.  Once the subject is walking at normal speed, say “turn and stop.”  Count the 
number of steps from “turn” until the subject is stable.  Imbalance may be indicated by wide stance, extra 
stepping or trunk motion. 
13. STEP OVER OBSTACLES 
Place the box (9 inches or 23 cm height) 10 feet away from where the subject will begin walking. Two 
shoeboxes taped together works well to create this apparatus. 
14. TIMED UP & GO WITH DUAL 
TASK 
Use the TUG time to determine the effects of dual tasking. The subject should walk a 3 meter distance. 
TUG: Have the subject sitting with the subject’s back against the chair. The subject will be timed from the 
moment you say “Go” until the subject returns to sitting. Stop timing when the subject’s buttocks hit the 
chair bottom and the subject’s back is against the chair. The chair should be firm without arms. TUG 
With Dual Task: While sitting determine how fast and accurately the subject can count backwards by 
threes starting from a number between 100-90. Then, ask the subject to count from a different number 
and after a few numbers say “Go”. Time the subject from the moment you say “Go” until the subject 
returns to the sitting position. Score dual task as affecting counting or walking if speed slows (>10%) 
from TUG and or new signs of imbalance.

1.13  FATIGUE 
Over the past week, have you usually felt fatigued?  This feeling is not part of being 
sleepy or sad. 
0: Normal: 
No fatigue. 
1: Slight: 
Fatigue occurs. However it does not cause me troubles doing 
things or being with people. 
2: Mild: 
Fatigue causes me some troubles doing things or being with 
people. 
3: Moderate: 
Fatigue causes me a lot of troubles doing things or being with 
people.  However, it does not stop me from doing anything. 
4:  Severe: 
Fatigue stops me from doing things or being with people. 
SCORE 
 
Part II: Motor Aspects of Experiences of Daily Living (M-EDL) 
2.1  SPEECH 
Over the past week, have you had problems with your speech? 
0: Normal: 
Not at all (no problems). 
1: Slight: 
My speech is soft, slurred or uneven, but it does not cause others 
to ask me to repeat myself. 
2: Mild: 
My speech causes people to ask me to occasionally repeat 
myself, but not every day. 
3: Moderate: 
My speech is unclear enough that others ask me to repeat myself 
every day even though most of my speech is understood. 
4: Severe: 
Most or all of my speech cannot be understood.

2.2 SALIVA AND DROOLING 
Over the past week, have you usually had too much saliva during when you are 
awake or when you sleep? 
0: Normal: 
Not at all (no problems). 
1: Slight: 
I have too much saliva, but do not drool. 
2: Mild: 
I have some drooling during sleep, but none when I am awake. 
3: Moderate: 
I have some drooling when I am awake, but I usually do not need 
tissues or a handkerchief. 
4: Severe: 
I have so much drooling that I regularly need to use tissues or a 
handkerchief to protect my clothes. 
SCORE 
 
2.3  CHEWING AND SWALLOWING 
Over the past week, have you usually had problems swallowing pills or eating meals? 
Do you need your pills cut or crushed or your meals to be made soft, chopped, or 
blended to avoid choking? 
0: Normal: 
No problems. 
1: Slight: 
I am aware of slowness in my chewing or increased effort at 
swallowing, but I do not choke or need to have my food specially 
prepared. 
2: Mild: 
I need to have my pills cut or my food specially prepared because 
of chewing or swallowing problems, but I have not choked over 
the past week. 
3: Moderate. 
I choked at least once in the past week. 
4: Severe: 
Because of chewing and swallowing problems, I need a feeding 
tube.

2: Mild 
I have a lot of trouble turning and need occasional help from 
someone else. 
3: Moderate: 
To turn over I often need help from someone else. 
4: Severe: 
I am unable to turn over without help from someone else. 
SCORE

Over the past week, have you usually had shaking or tremor? 
0: Normal:

1: Slight: 
Shaking or tremor occurs but does not cause problems with any

Shaking or tremor causes problems with only a few activities. 
3: Moderate: Shaking or tremor causes problems with many of my daily

1: Slight: 
I am slow or awkward, but I usually can do it on my first try.

I need more than one try to get up or need occasional help.

1: Slight:

and may have mentioned problems that you may never develop at all.  Not all patients develop all 
these problems, but because they can occur, it is important to ask all the questions to every 
patient. Thank you for your time and attention in completing this questionnaire.

Part III: Motor Examination 
 
 
 
Overview: This portion of the scale assesses the motor signs of PD. In administering Part III of the MDS-UPDRS 
the examiner should comply with the following guidelines: 
 
At the top of the form, mark whether the patient is on medication for treating the symptoms of Parkinson's disease 
and, if on levodopa, the time since the last dose. 
 
Also, if the patient is receiving medication for treating the symptoms of Parkinson’s disease, mark the patient’s 
clinical state using the following definitions: 
ON is the typical functional state when patients are receiving medication and have a good response. 
OFF is the typical functional state when patients have a poor response in spite of taking medications. 
 
The investigator should “rate what you see.” Admittedly, concurrent medical problems such as stroke, paralysis, 
arthritis, contracture, and orthopedic problems such as hip or knee replacement and scoliosis may interfere with 
individual items in the motor examination. In situations where it is absolutely impossible to test (e.g., amputations, 
plegia, limb in a cast), use the notation “UR” for Unable to Rate. Otherwise, rate the performance of each task as the 
patient performs in the context of co-morbidities. 
 
All items must have an integer rating (no half points, no missing ratings). 
 
Specific instructions are provided for the testing of each item. These should be followed in all instances. The 
investigator demonstrates while describing tasks the patient is to perform and rates function immediately thereafter. 
For Global Spontaneous Movement and Rest Tremor items (3.14 and 3.17), these items have been placed 
purposefully at the end of the scale because clinical information pertinent to the score will be obtained throughout the 
entire examination. 
 
At the end of the rating, indicate if dyskinesia (chorea or dystonia) was present at the time of the examination, and if 
so, whether these movements interfered with the motor examination. 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
3a  Is the patient on medication for treating the symptoms of Parkinson’s disease? 
 No  Yes 
 
 
 
 
3b 
If the patient is receiving medication for treating the symptoms of Parkinson’s disease, 
mark the patient’s clinical state using the following definitions: 
 
 ON: On is the typical functional state when patients are receiving medication and have a good response. 
 
 OFF: Off is the typical functional state when patients have a poor response in spite of taking medications. 
 
 
 
3c  Is the patient on levodopa ? 
 No  Yes

3.7 TOE TAPPING 
Instructions to examiner: Have the patient sit in a straight-backed chair with arms, both feet on the floor. 
Test each foot separately. Demonstrate the task, but do not continue to perform the task while the 
patient is being tested. Instruct the patient to place the heel on the ground in a comfortable position and 
then tap the toes 10 times as big and as fast as possible. Rate each side separately, evaluating speed, 
amplitude, hesitations, halts, and decrementing amplitude. 
0:   Normal: 
No problems. 
1: Slight: 
Any of the following: a) the regular rhythm is broken with one or two interruptions 
or hesitations of the tapping movement; b) slight slowing; c) amplitude 
decrements near the end of the ten taps. 
2: Mild: 
Any of the following: a) 3 to 5 interruptions during the tapping movements; b) mild 
slowing; c) amplitude decrements midway in the task. 
3: Moderate: 
Any of the following: a) more than 5 interruptions during the tapping movements 
or at least one longer arrest (freeze) in ongoing movement; b) moderate slowing; 
c) amplitude decrements after the 1st tap. 
4: Severe: 
Cannot or can only barely perform the task because of slowing, interruptions or 
decrements. 
SCORE 
 
R 
 
L 
3.8 LEG AGILITY 
Instructions to examiner: Have the patient sit in a straight-backed chair with arms. The patient should 
have both feet comfortably on the floor. Test each leg separately. Demonstrate the task, but do not 
continue to perform the task while the patient is being tested. Instruct the patient to place the foot on the 
ground in a comfortable position and then raise and stomp the foot on the ground 10 times as high and 
as fast as possible. Rate each side separately, evaluating speed, amplitude, hesitations, halts and 
decrementing amplitude. 
0: Normal: 
No problems. 
1: Slight: 
Any of the following: a) the regular rhythm is broken with one or two interruptions 
or hesitations of the movement; b) slight slowing; c) amplitude decrements near 
the end of the task. 
2: Mild: 
Any of the following: a) 3 to 5 interruptions during the movements; b) mild 
slowness; c) amplitude decrements midway in the task. 
3: Moderate:     Any of the following: a) more than 5 interruptions during the movement or at 
least one longer arrest (freeze) in ongoing movement; b) moderate slowing in 
speed; c) amplitude decrements after the 1st tap. 
4: Severe: 
    Cannot or can only barely perform the task because of slowing, interruptions,        
                            or decrements.
