3.9 ARISING FROM CHAIR 
Instructions to examiner: Have the patient sit in a straight-backed chair with arms, with both feet on the 
floor and sitting back in the chair (if the patient is not too short). Ask the patient to cross his/her arms 
across the chest and then to stand up. If the patient is not successful, repeat this attempt up to a 
maximum of two more times. If still unsuccessful, allow the patient to move forward in the chair to arise 
with arms folded across the chest. Allow only one attempt in this situation. If unsuccessful, allow the 
patient to push off using his/her hands on the arms of the chair. Allow a maximum of three trials of 
pushing off. If still not successful, assist the patient to arise. After the patient stands up, observe the 
posture for item 3.13. 
0: Normal: 
No problems. Able to arise quickly without hesitation. 
1: Slight:              Arising is slower than normal; or may need more than one attempt; or may 
need to move forward in the chair to arise. No need to use the arms of the 
chair. 
2: Mild: 
Pushes self up from the arms of the chair without difficulty. 
3: Moderate: 
Needs to push off, but tends to fall back; or may have to try more than one time 
using the arms of the chair, but can get up without help. 
4: Severe: 
Unable to arise without help. 
SCORE 
 
3.10 GAIT 
Instructions to examiner: Testing gait is best performed by having the patient walking away from and 
towards the examiner so that both right and left sides of the body can be easily observed 
simultaneously. The patient should walk at least 10 meters (30 feet), then turn around and return to 
the examiner. This item measures multiple behaviors: stride amplitude, stride speed, height of foot lift, 
heel strike during walking, turning, and arm swing, but not freezing. Assess also for “freezing of gait” 
(next item 3.11) while patient is walking. Observe posture for item 3.13. 
0: Normal: 
No problems. 
1: Slight: 
Independent walking with minor gait impairment. 
2: Mild: 
Independent walking but with substantial gait impairment. 
3: Moderate: 
Requires an assistance device for safe walking (walking stick, walker) but not a 
person. 
4: Severe: 
Cannot walk at all or only with another person’s assistance. 
 
Page 23 
 
 
Last updated August 13, 2019 | Copyright © 2008 International Parkinson and Movement Disorder Society. All rights reserved 
This scale may not be copied, distributed or otherwise used in whole or in part without prior written consent of the International Parkinson and Movement Disorder Society 
 
 
3.11 FREEZING OF GAIT 
Instructions to examiner:  While assessing gait, also assess for the presence of any gait freezing 
episodes. Observe for start hesitation and stuttering movements especially when turning and reaching 
the end of the task. To the extent that safety permits, patients may NOT use sensory tricks during the 
assessment. 
0: Normal: 
No freezing. 
1: Slight:            Freezes on starting, turning, or walking through doorway with a single halt during 
any of these events, but then continues smoothly without freezing during straight 
walking. 
2: Mild: 
 Freezes on starting, turning, or walking through doorway with more than one halt 
during any of these activities, but continues smoothly without freezing during 
straight walking. 
3: Moderate: 
Freezes once during straight walking. 
4: Severe: 
Freezes multiple times during straight walking. 
SCORE 
 
3.12 POSTURAL STABILITY 
Instructions to examiner: The test examines the response to sudden body displacement produced by a 
quick, forceful pull on the shoulders while the patient is standing erect with eyes open and feet 
comfortably apart and parallel to each other. Test retropulsion. Stand behind the patient and instruct 
the patient on what is about to happen. Explain that s/he is allowed to take a step backwards to avoid 
falling. There should be a solid wall behind the examiner, at least 1-2 meters away to allow for the 
observation of the number of retropulsive steps. The first pull is an instructional demonstration and is 
purposely milder and not rated. The second time the shoulders are pulled briskly and forcefully towards 
the examiner with enough force to displace the center of gravity so that patient MUST take a step 
backwards. The examiner needs to be ready to catch the patient, but must stand sufficiently back so as 
to allow enough room for the patient to take several steps to recover independently. Do not allow the 
patient to flex the body abnormally forward in anticipation of the pull. Observe for the number of steps 
backwards or falling. Up to and including two steps for recovery is considered normal, so abnormal 
ratings begin with three steps. If the patient fails to understand the test, the examiner can repeat the 
test so that the rating is based on an assessment that the examiner feels reflects the patient’s limitations 
rather than misunderstanding or lack of preparedness. Observe standing posture for item 3.13. 
0: Normal: 
No problems. Recovers with one or two steps. 
1: Slight: 
3-5 steps, but subject recovers unaided. 
2: Mild: 
More than 5 steps, but subject recovers unaided. 
3: Moderate: 
Stands safely, but with absence of postural response; falls if not caught by 
examiner. 
4: Severe: 
Very unstable, tends to lose balance spontaneously or with just a gentle pull on 
the shoulders. 
 
==PAGE:118==
Page 24 
 
 
Last updated August 13, 2019 | Copyright © 2008 International Parkinson and Movement Disorder Society. All rights reserved 
This scale may not be copied, distributed or otherwise used in whole or in part without prior written consent of the International Parkinson and Movement Disorder Society 
 
 
3.13 POSTURE 
Instructions to examiner: Posture is assessed with the patient standing erect after arising from a chair, 
during walking, and while being tested for postural reflexes. If you notice poor posture, tell the patient to 
stand up straight and see if the posture improves (see option 2 below). Rate the worst posture seen in 
these three observation points. Observe for flexion and side-to-side leaning. 
0: Normal: 
No problems. 
1: Slight: 
Not quite erect, but posture could be normal for older person. 
2: Mild: 
Definite flexion, scoliosis or leaning to one side, but patient can correct posture 
to normal posture when asked to do so. 
3:  Moderate: 
Stooped posture, scoliosis or leaning to one side that cannot be corrected 
volitionally to a normal posture by the patient. 
4:   Severe: 
Flexion, scoliosis or leaning with extreme abnormality of posture. 
SCORE 
 
3.14 GLOBAL SPONTANEITY OF MOVEMENT (BODY BRADYKINESIA) 
Instructions to examiner: This global rating combines all observations on slowness, hesitancy, and 
small amplitude and poverty of movement in general, including a reduction of gesturing and of crossing 
the legs. This assessment is based on the examiner’s global impression after observing for 
spontaneous gestures while sitting, and the nature of arising and walking. 
0: Normal: 
No problems. 
1: Slight: 
Slight global slowness and poverty of spontaneous movements. 
2: Mild: 
Mild global slowness and poverty of spontaneous movements. 
3: Moderate: 
Moderate global slowness and poverty of spontaneous movements. 
4:  Severe: 
Severe global slowness and poverty of spontaneous movements. 
 
3.15 POSTURAL TREMOR OF THE HANDS 
 Instructions to examiner: All tremor, including re-emergent rest tremor, that is present in this posture is 
to be included in this rating. Rate each hand separately. Rate the highest amplitude seen. Instruct the 
patient to stretch the arms out in front of the body with palms down. The wrist should be straight and 
the fingers comfortably separated so that they do not touch each other. Observe this posture for 10 
seconds. 
0: Normal: 
No tremor. 
1: Slight: 
Tremor is present but less than 1 cm in amplitude. 
2: Mild: 
Tremor is at least 1 but less than 3 cm in amplitude. 
3: Moderate: 
Tremor is at least 3 but less than 10 cm in amplitude. 
4:  Severe: 
Tremor is at least 10 cm in amplitude.

# Function to calculate the sample size required to reach 80% power
4
power_sample_size_required <- function(data_m, diff_col, confounders , power_
target = 0.8, max_sample_size = 400, step = 5, n_sims = 2) {
5
6
# Build formulas
7
if (length(confounders) > 0) {
8
confounder_formula <- paste(confounders , collapse = " + ")
9
formula <- as.formula(paste(diff_col, "~ Condition +", confounder_formula)
)
10
formula_bis <- as.formula(paste("response ~ Condition +", confounder_
formula))
11
} else {
12
formula <- as.formula(paste(diff_col, "~ Condition"))
13
formula_bis <- as.formula("response ~ Condition")
14
}
15
16
my.structure <- data_m %>%
17
# rename(Condition = Treatment) %>%
18
mutate(
19
Condition = ifelse(Condition == "Control", 0, 1),
20
Relative_ID = as.factor(Relative_ID),
21
Sequence = as.factor(Sequence)
22
)
23
24
# Fit initial model to get effect size and variance components
25
model <- lme(formula , random = ~1 | Relative_ID, data = my.structure)
26
effect_size <- summary(model)$tTable[2, 1]
# beta for Condition
27
params <- fixef(model)
28
alpha <- params["(Intercept)"]
29
beta1 <- params["Condition"]
30
beta_confounders <- params[names(params) %in% confounders]
31
beta2 <- if ("Sequence2" %in% names(params)) params["Sequence2"] else 0
32
33
re_sd <- as.numeric(VarCorr(model)[, "StdDev"])
34
eta <- re_sd[1]
# random intercept SD
35
sigma <- re_sd[2] # residual SD
36

# Function to simulate data and compute power at given sample size
38
simulate_power <- function(sample_size) {
39
temp_results <- numeric(n_sims)
40
41
for (sim in 1:n_sims) {
42
# simulate new Relative_ID factor with sample_size unique IDs
43
new_IDs <- factor(1:sample_size)
44
45
# simulate Condition and other confounders proportionally from original
data
46
sim_data <- data.frame(
47
Relative_ID = rep(new_IDs, each = nrow(my.structure) / length(unique(
my.structure$Relative_ID))),
48
Condition = rep(sample(my.structure$Condition , sample_size, replace =
TRUE), each = nrow(my.structure) / length(unique(my.structure$
Relative_ID))),
104
iGRASP
==PAGE:121==
49
Sequence = rep(sample(my.structure$Sequence , sample_size, replace =
TRUE), each = nrow(my.structure) / length(unique(my.structure$
Relative_ID)))
50
)
51
52
# Add confounders with random sampling if any
53
if (length(confounders) > 0) {
54
for (cf in confounders) {
55
sim_data[[cf]] <- rep(sample(my.structure[[cf]], sample_size,
replace = TRUE), each = nrow(my.structure) / length(unique(my.
structure$Relative_ID)))
56
}
57
}
58
59
n_rows <- nrow(sim_data)
60
n_ids <- length(unique(sim_data$Relative_ID))
61
62
# Simulate random effects and residuals
63
z <- rnorm(n_ids)
64
epsilon <- rnorm(n_rows)
65
66
# Compute response
67
sim_data$response <- NA
68
for (i in 1:n_rows) {
69
id_num <- as.numeric(sim_data$Relative_ID[i])
70
cond <- sim_data$Condition[i]
71
seq_val <- as.numeric(sim_data$Sequence[i])
72
confounder_effect <- 0
73
if (length(beta_confounders) > 0) {
74
for (cf in names(beta_confounders)) {
75
confounder_effect <- confounder_effect + beta_confounders[cf] *
sim_data[[cf]][i]
76
}
77
}
78
sim_data$response[i] <- alpha +
79
effect_size * cond +
80
beta2 * seq_val +
81
confounder_effect +
82
eta * z[id_num] +
83
sigma * epsilon[i]
84
}
85
86
# Fit model on simulated data
87
fit <- try(lme(formula_bis, random = ~1 | Relative_ID, data = sim_data),
silent = TRUE)
88
if (inherits(fit, "try-error")) {
89
temp_results[sim] <- 0
90
} else {
91
p_val <- summary(fit)$tTable["Condition", "p-value"]
92
temp_results[sim] <- as.numeric(p_val < 0.05)
93
}
94
}
95
mean(temp_results)
96
}
97
98
# Search for smallest sample size with power >= power_target
99
sample_sizes <- seq(10, max_sample_size, by = step)
100
power_values <- sapply(sample_sizes, simulate_power)
101
102
if (all(power_values < power_target)) {
103
warning("Power target not reached within max_sample_size")
104
return(NA)
iGRASP
105
==PAGE:122==
105
}
106
107
# Find minimum sample size achieving power target
108
min_index <- which(power_values >= power_target)[1]
109
sample_size_required <- sample_sizes[min_index]
110
return(sample_size_required)
111
}

power_calculation_function_conf <- function(data_m, diff_col, diff_col_renamed
, confounders) {

if (length(confounders) > 0) {
116

Condition = ifelse(Condition == "Control", 0, 1),

132

136

142

143
sigma <- re_sd[2]
144

## Dynamic intervention range based on effect size
146
scaling_factor <- 2
147
step_fraction <- min(0.02, max(0.001, abs(size_effect) * 0.01))
148
range_width <- max(2, abs(size_effect) * scaling_factor)
149
step_size <- max(0.05, abs(size_effect) * step_fraction)
150

# Make sure the range is symmetric around 0
152
my.intervention <- seq(-range_width, range_width, by = step_size)
153
my.power <- numeric(length(my.intervention))
154
number.of.sims <- 1 ## Increase for actual power estimation
155
156
for (j in seq_along(my.intervention)) {
157

158
159
for (k in 1:number.of.sims) {
160
my.z <- rnorm(length(unique(my.structure$Relative_ID)))
161
my.epsilon <- rnorm(nrow(my.structure))
162
my.structure$response <- NA
# initialize
163

.structure[[cf]][i]

plot_fixed_effects_and_boxplots <- function(models ,formulas , pdf_path, boxplot
_data_list,boxplot_data_list_old, variable_names) {

)))) {

names[i]))

names[i], e$message))

})

# fixed_formula <- tryCatch(update(formula(model), . ~ . + Relative_ID),

analyze_file <- function(filepath , type, path_to_daily_data, confounders ,

374

377

filter(Relative_ID != 5)}

# Combine all terms

)

sep = ""))
449

# Check if all relevant factors have at least 2 levels
iGRASP

==PAGE:132==
452

nlevels(droplevels(df[[var]])) >= 2

} else {

TRUE

}

# Compliance function

compliance_function <- function(df, days_df, disease) {
17
# Step 1: Filter baseline rows with intervention info
18
baseline <- df %>%
19
filter(redcap_event_name == "baseline_arm_1", !is.na(intervention)) %>%
20
select(-`Total Time Period 1`, -`Total Time Period 2`)
21
iGRASP

==PAGE:162==
22

23

24
25
# Step 3: Reshape to long format and compute usage
26

27
pivot_longer(cols = starts_with("Total Time"), names_to = "Period", values
_to = "Usage") %>%
28
pivot_longer(cols = starts_with("Days of Use"), names_to = "Day_Period",
values_to = "Days") %>%
29
filter((Period == "Total Time Period 1" & Day_Period == "Days of Use
Period 1") |
30
(Period == "Total Time Period 2" & Day_Period == "Days of Use
Period 2")) %>%
31
mutate(
32
Condition = case_when(
33
intervention == 1 & Period == "Total Time Period 1" ~ "Control",
34
intervention == 1 & Period == "Total Time Period 2" ~ "Intervention",
35
intervention == 2 & Period == "Total Time Period 1" ~ "Intervention",
36
intervention == 2 & Period == "Total Time Period 2" ~ "Control",
37
TRUE ~ NA_character_
38
),
39
Usage_per_day = ifelse(Days == 0, NA, Usage / Days)
40
) %>%
41
filter(!is.na(Usage_per_day)) %>%
42
group_by(Relative_ID) %>%
43
filter(n() == 2) %>%
44
ungroup()
45
46
# Mixed-effects models
47
model_raw <- lmer(Usage ~ Condition + (1 | Relative_ID), data = long_df)
48
model_norm <- lmer(Usage_per_day ~ Condition + (1 | Relative_ID), data =
long_df)

# Plot 1: Average total usage by condition

y = "Total Usage (min)", x = ""

62

y = "Usage per Day (min)", x = ""

) +

75

ggsave(paste0("C:/Users/emmie/OneDrive - Danmarks Tekniske Universitet/

insole_usage_", disease , ".png"),

plot = p1_norm, width = 8, height = 6, bg = "white")

D.4
Carry-over Effect Analysis Details
Based on the structure illustrated in Table D.1, the total outcome across both periods
for each subject can be derived and compared between treatment sequences to assess
potential carryover effects. The model includes the overall mean (µ), period effects (πk
for k = 1, 2), direct effects of intervention and control (ϕInt, ϕCtrl), and carryover effects
of intervention and control (λInt, λCtrl). The outcome in each period for each sequence is
denoted by Yp,s. The ”IC” sequence corresponds to Intervention in Period 1 and Control
in Period 2, while ”CI” refers to the reverse. The ”Sum” column shows the total outcome
across both periods for each sequence, while the ”Difference” column highlights the within-
subject variation between periods.
Table D.1: Treatment Effects in Both Periods by Sequence (Intervention vs Control)
Period 1 Y1
Period 2 Y2
Sum
Difference
Sequence IC
µ + π1 + ϕInt
µ + π2 + ϕCtrl + λInt
Y1,IC + Y2,IC
Y1,IC −Y2,IC
Sequence CI
µ + π1 + ϕCtrl
µ + π2 + ϕInt + λCtrl
Y1,CI + Y2,CI
Y1,CI −Y2,CI
iGRASP
151
==PAGE:168==
To test the null hypothesis:
H0 : λInt = λCtrl
the total (sum) outcome per subject was used as the dependent variable. The following
linear mixed-effects model is fit:
Sumij ∼Sequencei ∗Confoundersi + (1 | Subjectj)
where:
• Sumij is the total outcome for subject j in sequence i (i.e., Y1 + Y2),
• Sequencei indicates the treatment order (IC or CI),
• Confoundersi are covariates such as age, baseline score, or time since injury,
• Sequencei ∗Confoundersi models both main and interaction effects,
• (1 | Subjectj) is a random intercept to account for individual differences.
The fixed effect estimate for Condition allows us to test whether the sum outcomes differ
between sequences, which would suggest the presence of a carryover effect (λ1 ̸= λ2).
This model is implemented in R using the nlme::lme() function.

Step 3: Iteratively Remove Other Non-significant Terms
Non-significant interaction and main effects were sequentially removed from the full model
based on their p-values. The terms were excluded in the following order: interaction
between Condition and h_y_score, h_y_score, interaction between Condition and red-
cap_event_name, interaction between Condition and age, interaction between Condition
and Sequence, interaction between Condition and gender, Sequence, redcap_event_name,
and age. All substeps are illustrated in the figures below.
